/></a></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;text-align:center;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Washington_series" title="Template:Washington series"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Washington_series" title="Template talk:Washington series"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Washington_series&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>George Washington</b> (February 22, 1732<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-calendar_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calendar-5">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 <span class="nowrap">December 14</span>, 1799) was one of the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a> and served as the nation\xe2\x80\x99s first President (1789\xe2\x80\x931797). In the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>,  General Washington led Patriot forces to victory over the British and their allies. He presided at the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention</a> of 1787 which established the new federal government, and he has been called the "<a href="/wiki/Father_of_the_nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Father of the nation">Father of His Country</a>"\n</p><p>Washington was born to a moderately prosperous family of planters and slaveholders in <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">colonial Virginia</a>. He had early educational opportunities, learned mathematics, and soon launched a successful career as a surveyor which enabled him to make significant land investments. He then became a key leader of the Virginia militia in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>. He was appointed commander-in-chief of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> during the Revolutionary War, leading an allied campaign to victory at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">Siege of Yorktown</a> which ended the conflict. His devotion to <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">Republicanism</a> and revulsion for tyrannical power impelled him to decline further authority after victory, and he resigned as commander-in-chief in 1783.\n</p><p>As the country\xe2\x80\x99s premier statesman, Washington was unanimously elected President by the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> in the first two national elections. He promoted and oversaw implementation of a strong, well-financed national government, using the economic plans of his Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, while remaining impartial in the fierce rivalry between Hamilton and  Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>. When the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> plunged Europe into war, Washington proclaimed a policy of neutrality to protect American ships, while the controversial <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> of 1795 normalized trade relations with Great Britain. He set numerous precedents that have endured, such as the Cabinet advisory system, the inaugural address, and the title "Mr. President". His seminal <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address" title="George Washington&#39;s Farewell Address">Farewell Address</a> strongly warned against political partisanship, sectionalism, and involvement in foreign wars. \n</p><p>Washington owned slaves throughout his life from age 11, but he became increasingly troubled by slavery and freed his slaves in his will. He was a member of the Anglican church and the Freemasons, and he urged tolerance for all religions in his roles as general and President.   Upon his death, he was famously eulogized as "first in war, first in peace, and first in the hearts of his countrymen". Washington has been widely memorialized by monuments, art, places, stamps and currency, and he has been ranked by scholars among the top four American Presidents.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span id="Early_years_.281732.E2.80.931752.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years_(1732\xe2\x80\x931752)">Early years (1732\xe2\x80\x931752)</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ancestry_of_George_Washington" title="Ancestry of George Washington">Ancestry of George Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">Colony of Virginia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/British_America" title="British America">British America</a></div>\n<p>George Washington was born February 22, 1732, the first child of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_Washington" title="Augustine Washington">Augustine Washington</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ball_Washington" title="Mary Ball Washington">Mary Ball Washington</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Wakefield,_Virginia" title="Wakefield, Virginia">Wakefield</a> on their <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Birthplace_National_Monument" title="George Washington Birthplace National Monument">Popes Creek Estate</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">Colony of Virginia</a>. He was then a subject of the British Empire, under the reign of <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George II</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling20023Chernow20105\xe2\x80\x937_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20023Chernow20105\xe2\x80\x937-6">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> descended primarily from the gentry of <a href="/wiki/Sulgrave" title="Sulgrave">Sulgrave</a>, England. His great-grandfather <a href="/wiki/John_Washington" title="John Washington">John Washington</a> emigrated to Virginia in 1656. He was a tobacco planter who accumulated land and slaves, as did his son <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Washington_(1659%E2%80%931698)" title="Lawrence Washington (1659\xe2\x80\x931698)">Lawrence</a> and his grandson Augustine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow20103-5_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20103-5-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington was reared in the rich open farmlands of Virginia\'s <a href="/wiki/Tidewater_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Tidewater region">Tidewater region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow20106\xe2\x80\x937Alden19932,_26Randall199717_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20106\xe2\x80\x937Alden19932,_26Randall199717-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> His father Augustine was a moderately wealthy planter, justice of the peace, and county sheriff who had 10 children, four by his first marriage to Jane Butler and six by his second marriage to Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20022Hofstra1998viiAlden19933\xe2\x80\x934Wiencek200354_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20022Hofstra1998viiAlden19933\xe2\x80\x934Wiencek200354-9">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Washington was one of seven surviving children, including older half-brothers <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Washington_(1718%E2%80%931752)" title="Lawrence Washington (1718\xe2\x80\x931752)">Lawrence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustine_Washington,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Augustine Washington, Jr.">Augustine</a>, and full siblings <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Washington" title="Samuel Washington">Samuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Betty_Washington_Lewis" title="Betty Washington Lewis">Elizabeth (Betty)</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Augustine_Washington" title="John Augustine Washington">John Augustine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Washington" title="Charles Washington">Charles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling20023_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20023-10">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At age 3, Washington and his family moved to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vernon" title="Mount Vernon">Epsewasson</a>, a 2,500-acre plantation which his father purchased on the bluffs of the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac River</a>; when Washington was 6 they moved to <a href="/wiki/Ferry_Farm" title="Ferry Farm">Ferry Farm</a> near <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling20024Chernow20107\xe2\x80\x938_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20024Chernow20107\xe2\x80\x938-13">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He spent much of his boyhood there, and it is said to be the setting for the <a href="/wiki/Ferry_Farm#Cherry_Tree" title="Ferry Farm">cherry tree</a> legend authored by Parson Weems. When Washington\'s father, then age 48, died of a sudden illness on April 12, 1743, 11 year-old George was then kept under the strict care of his mother Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow20108\xe2\x80\x9310_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20108\xe2\x80\x9310-14">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He inherited Ferry Farm and 10 slaves, while his older half-brother Lawrence inherited Epsewasson and changed its name to Mount Vernon in honor of British Vice Admiral <a href="/wiki/Edward_Vernon" title="Edward Vernon">Edward Vernon</a>, his commander during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Jenkins%27_Ear" title="War of Jenkins&#39; Ear">War of Jenkins\' Ear</a>.\n</p><p>Washington\'s first teachers were his father and Lawrence; his total education spanned over 8 years. His mother could not afford the cost of England\'s <a href="/wiki/Appleby_Grammar_School" title="Appleby Grammar School">Appleby Grammar School</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200214Chernow201011\xe2\x80\x9312_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200214Chernow201011\xe2\x80\x9312-15">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> so he was tutored by various masters including Mr. Hobby, his father\xe2\x80\x99s former tenant; he also attended the Fredericksburg school of Anglican clergyman James Mayre.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnott20051\xe2\x80\x935Ferling20105\xe2\x80\x936Ferling200214Chernow201011\xe2\x80\x9312_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnott20051\xe2\x80\x935Ferling20105\xe2\x80\x936Ferling200214Chernow201011\xe2\x80\x9312-16">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> He was taught mathematics, trigonometry, and surveying by school master Henry Williams, and he had a natural talent in draftsmanship and map-making.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201011\xe2\x80\x9312_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201011\xe2\x80\x9312-17">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He purchased books on military affairs, agriculture, and history, as well as popular novels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510-18">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> By early adulthood, he was able to write with precision and considerable force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201012_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201012-19">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s mother rejected a plan for him to join the Royal Navy in September 1746 when he was 14.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:199Chernow201016\xe2\x80\x9318_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:199Chernow201016\xe2\x80\x9318-20">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> His half-brother Lawrence had married Anne Fairfax, the daughter of powerful Virginia statesman <a href="/wiki/William_Fairfax" title="William Fairfax">William Fairfax</a>, in 1743, and he became Washington\'s idolized surrogate father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20022Chernow201010_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20022Chernow201010-21">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Washington moved to Mount Vernon with Lawrence and Ann when he was 17.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20022Alden19934\xe2\x80\x935,_73Chernow201010\xe2\x80\x9314,_pp=15\xe2\x80\x9316_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20022Alden19934\xe2\x80\x935,_73Chernow201010\xe2\x80\x9314,_pp=15\xe2\x80\x9316-22">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Washington was close friends with William Fairfax\'s son <a href="/wiki/George_William_Fairfax" title="George William Fairfax">George</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall199736_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199736-23">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> whose wife <a href="/wiki/Sally_Fairfax" title="Sally Fairfax">Sally</a> had been an early romantic interest; she maintained correspondence with Washington when she moved to England with her father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington and George William Fairfax accompanied surveyor James Genn in 1748 when Washington was 16. Genn had been sent out by Lord Fairfax to survey the Shenandoah lands, and Washington gained valuable experience during the month-long trip.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510Chernow201019_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510Chernow201019-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> He received a commission and surveyor\'s license from the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> in 1749 when he was 17, and he was appointed surveyor of Culpepper, Virginia due to Fairfax\'s influence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510Chernow201022_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510Chernow201022-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> He primarily surveyed for Lord Fairfax in the Blue Ridge Mountains, after a preliminary survey of eastern Culpepper County.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall199754\xe2\x80\x9355Chernow201022\xe2\x80\x9323_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199754\xe2\x80\x9355Chernow201022\xe2\x80\x9323-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> He made repeated surveys of the Shenandoah Valley during the spring of 1750, and he became accustomed to the wilderness. He bought almost 1,500 acres in the Shenandoah Valley in October 1750, his first large land investment, and he accumulated 2,315 acres in the Shenandoah Valley by the time he was 18, when he resigned his commission of Culpepper County surveyor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201023_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201023-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington stopped surveying within a few years but he continued purchasing land. He acquired more than 70,000 acres in seven states and the <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a> over the course of his lifetime; it took him 25 years to expand his Mount Vernon estate from 2,000 to 8,000 acres.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;George_Washington&#39;s_Aggressive_Real_Estate_Investment&quot;_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;George_Washington&#39;s_Aggressive_Real_Estate_Investment&quot;-29">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> He bought more parcels of land to spur development around Federal City (Washington, DC). Rather than selling multiple lots to large investors, he sold individual lots to middle income investors, believing that they were more likely to make committed improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010704\xe2\x80\x93705_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010704\xe2\x80\x93705-30">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1751, Washington made his only trip abroad with Lawrence to <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> in the hope that the climate would be beneficial to his brother\'s tuberculosis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201024_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201024-31">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> During the trip, Washington contracted smallpox which immunized him but left his face slightly scarred.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlexner19748_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner19748-32">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Lawrence\'s health continued to decline and he returned to Mount Vernon, where he died on July 26, 1752.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:264Chernow201026_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:264Chernow201026-33">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Washington inherited his Mount Vernon estate after the deaths of Lawrence\'s wife Ann and their daughter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:15\xe2\x80\x9372Chernow201026_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:15\xe2\x80\x9372Chernow201026-34">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>\n<p>Washington was generally reserved and somewhat taciturn, yet he had a strong presence among company. He made speeches and announcements when required, but he was not known as an orator or a debater.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200216Randall199734,_436Chernow201029\xe2\x80\x9330_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200216Randall199734,_436Chernow201029\xe2\x80\x9330-35">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> He was taller than most of his contemporaries;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002-36">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> accounts of his height vary from 6&#160;ft (1.83&#160;m) to 6&#160;ft 3.5&#160;in (1.92&#160;m) tall, and he weighed between 210\xe2\x80\x93220 pounds as an adult.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200216Chernow201029\xe2\x80\x9330_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200216Chernow201029\xe2\x80\x9330-37">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He had wide hips, a slim waist, a broad chest, narrow shoulders, muscular thighs, and exceptionally large hands, and he was widely known for his great strength\xe2\x80\x94particularly in his long arms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010123-125_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010123-125-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> He had piercing grey-blue eyes, fair skin, and light reddish-brown hair, although he wore his hair powdered in the fashion of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201030_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201030-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He had a rugged and dominating presence which garnered respect from his male peers. However, he suffered frequently from severe tooth decay, and ultimately lost all his teeth but one. He had several sets of false teeth made which he went through during his presidency\xe2\x80\x94none of which were made of wood, contrary to common lore. These dental problems left him in constant pain for which he took <a href="/wiki/Laudanum" title="Laudanum">laudanum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201030,_290,_437\xe2\x80\x93439,_642\xe2\x80\x93643_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201030,_290,_437\xe2\x80\x93439,_642\xe2\x80\x93643-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> As a public figure, he demanded that his dentist keep his dental care absolutely private.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010642\xe2\x80\x93643_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010642\xe2\x80\x93643-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington was an above-average equestrian early in life. He collected thoroughbreds at Mount Vernon, and his two favorite horses were <a href="/wiki/Blueskin_(horse)" title="Blueskin (horse)">Blueskin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_(horse)" title="Nelson (horse)">Nelson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124,_469_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124,_469-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Fellow Virginian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> said that Washington was "the best horseman of his age and the most graceful figure that could be seen on horseback",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and he hunted foxes, deer, ducks, and other game.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010469_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010469-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> He was an also excellent dancer and attended the theater frequently. He drank in moderation but was morally opposed to excessive drinking, gambling, tobacco smoking, and profanity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010134_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010134-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Colonial_soldier_.281752.E2.80.931758.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_soldier_(1752\xe2\x80\x931758)">Colonial soldier (1752\xe2\x80\x931758)</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_in_the_French_and_Indian_War" title="George Washington in the French and Indian War">George Washington in the French and Indian War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Braddock_Expedition" title="Braddock Expedition">Braddock Expedition</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:beige"><center><i>Washington Expeditions 1753\xe2\x80\x931754</i></center></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Major_George_Washington_-_1754.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Major_George_Washington_-_1754.tif/lossy-page1-160px-Major_George_Washington_-_1754.tif.jpg" width="160" height="197" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Major_George_Washington_-_1754.tif/lossy-page1-240px-Major_George_Washington_-_1754.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Major_George_Washington_-_1754.tif/lossy-page1-320px-Major_George_Washington_-_1754.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="428" data-file-height="526" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><b>Major George Washington</b></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Pennsylvania_Mapb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Washington_Pennsylvania_Mapb.jpg/160px-Washington_Pennsylvania_Mapb.jpg" width="160" height="202" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Washington_Pennsylvania_Mapb.jpg/240px-Washington_Pennsylvania_Mapb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Washington_Pennsylvania_Mapb.jpg/320px-Washington_Pennsylvania_Mapb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4549" data-file-height="5742" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><b>Washington\'s Map</b></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>                              \n<p>The death of Washington\'s brother Lawrence left vacant his position as Adjutant General, and Washington began his pursuit of a military life inspired by Lawrence\'s service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:268Fitzpatrick1936510_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:268Fitzpatrick1936510-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> He was trained in musters and drills, then <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dinwiddie" title="Robert Dinwiddie">Robert Dinwiddie</a> appointed him adjutant to the Southern district in December 1752; he was subsequently appointed to the Northern and Eastern districts, as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510-18">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In February 1753, Dindwiddie appointed him to the rank of Major at an annual salary of 100 pounds, then appointed him as British military ambassador to the French officials and Indians as far north as Erie, Pennsylvania when Washington was 21. Thirty years later Washington reflected in amazement "that so young and inexperienced a person should have been employed" in such negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201026\xe2\x80\x9327,_31Randall199774_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201026\xe2\x80\x9327,_31Randall199774-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British government had ordered Dinwiddie to guard British territorial claims in the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> basin, protecting trade activity with the Indians and others in the various settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_510-18">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Dindwiddie ordered Washington to deliver a letter in late 1753 asking French commander <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Legardeur_de_Saint-Pierre" title="Jacques Legardeur de Saint-Pierre">Jacques Legardeur de Saint-Pierre</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Le_Boeuf" title="Fort Le Boeuf">Fort Le Boeuf</a> to vacate the Ohio Valley, and offering him a safe escort to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a>. Washington was also to make peace with the <a href="/wiki/Iroquois_Confederacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Iroquois Confederacy">Six Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:274\xe2\x80\x93327Chernow201033_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman19481:274\xe2\x80\x93327Chernow201033-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> He and six frontiersmen reached the Ohio River that November, but the French had withdrawn. He met with <a href="/wiki/Tanacharison" title="Tanacharison">Tanacharison</a> ("Half-King") and other Six Nations Iroquois chiefs at <a href="/wiki/Logstown" title="Logstown">Logstown</a> and secured their support against the French if needed, and then continued 60 miles (97&#160;km) and met the French at Venango\xe2\x80\x94but the letter was refused. Washington then reached Fort Le Boeuf and delivered the letter to the commander, who replied that Dinwiddie should send his demand to the Major General of New France at its capital in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELengel200523\xe2\x80\x9324Fitzpatrick1936510\xe2\x80\x93511,_v._19Chernow201033_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel200523\xe2\x80\x9324Fitzpatrick1936510\xe2\x80\x93511,_v._19Chernow201033-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Washington\xe2\x80\x99s diary of the expedition was printed by <a href="/wiki/William_Hunter_(publisher)" title="William Hunter (publisher)">William Hunter</a> according to Dinwiddie\xe2\x80\x99s order, giving Washington name recognition in Virginia and England;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511,_v._19_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511,_v._19-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> it also helped him obtain a commission to raise a company of men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrizzard200286Lengel2005xxiii_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard200286Lengel2005xxiii-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="French_and_Indian_War">French and Indian War</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a></div>\n<p>In 1753, the French military advanced into the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">Ohio Country</a>, territory that both France and Britain wanted to claim. Virginia\'s <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Company" title="Ohio Company">Ohio Company</a> was created to encourage British settlement of the land; it had an economic interest in the region, and both Dinwiddie and Washington were stockholders. The land which joined the Monongahela and Allegheny rivers (modern <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>) was highly prized by both nations,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_George_Washington_and_the_French_&amp;_Indian_War&quot;_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_George_Washington_and_the_French_&amp;_Indian_War&quot;-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and the competing stakes led to the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> (1754\xe2\x80\x9362) and contributed to the start of the global <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years\' War</a> (1756\xe2\x80\x9363). It was Washington who ordered the first shot fired in that war in 1754.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden199313_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199313-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG/220px-The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG/330px-The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG/440px-The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG 2x" data-file-width="897" data-file-height="605" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Night_Council_At_Fort_Necessity_from_the_Darlington_Collection_of_Engravings.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An engraved depiction of Lieutenant Colonel George Washington\'s evening council at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Necessity" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort Necessity">Fort Necessity</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On March 15, 1754, Governor Dinwiddie commissioned Washington Lieutenant Colonel in the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Regiment" title="Virginia Regiment">Virginia Regiment</a> at age 21 and sent him to confront French forces at Pittsburgh. Dindwiddie ordered Washington to take prisoners or kill those who resisted British control of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_George_Washington_and_the_French_&amp;_Indian_War&quot;_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_George_Washington_and_the_French_&amp;_Indian_War&quot;-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> He set out on April 2 with 150 men, and received news en route that a French force had driven out colonial traders and begun construction of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Duquesne" title="Fort Duquesne">Fort Duquesne</a>. Tanacharison and a few warriors discovered a small detachment of French troops east of <a href="/wiki/Uniontown,_Pennsylvania" title="Uniontown, Pennsylvania">Uniontown, Pennsylvania</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Coulon_de_Jumonville" title="Joseph Coulon de Jumonville">Joseph Coulon de Jumonville</a>, so Washington built an entrenched camp at Great Meadows which he called <a href="/wiki/Fort_Necessity_National_Battlefield" title="Fort Necessity National Battlefield">Fort Necessity</a>. He then led his unit and their <a href="/wiki/Mingo" title="Mingo">Mingo</a> allies in an ambush against the French on May 28 in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jumonville_Glen" title="Battle of Jumonville Glen">Battle of Jumonville Glen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511,_v._19_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511,_v._19-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> There are discrepancies, but primary accounts indicate that the battle lasted about 15 minutes. Jumonville was killed and most of his party were taken prisoner or killed, and Tanacharison nicknamed Washington the "Town Destroyer".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELengel200531\xe2\x80\x9338Anderson200053\xe2\x80\x9358Misencik2014131_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel200531\xe2\x80\x9338Anderson200053\xe2\x80\x9358Misencik2014131-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 1754, the French responded by attacking Fort Necessity in a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Necessity" title="Battle of Fort Necessity">ten-hour battle</a> which ended in Washington\'s only surrender and the return of his force to Virginia\xe2\x80\x94but only after he signed a falsely translated surrender document saying that he had "assassinated" Jumonville,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_511_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_511-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002115\xe2\x80\x9319Lengel200544_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002115\xe2\x80\x9319Lengel200544-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and this mistranslated document became the pretext to blame him for starting a war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_511_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_511-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Ellis" title="Joseph Ellis">Joseph Ellis</a> concludes that the episode demonstrated Washington\'s bravery and initiative, as well as his inexperience and impetuosity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200417\xe2\x80\x9318_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200417\xe2\x80\x9318-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Upon his return to Virginia, Washington refused to accept a demotion to the rank of captain and resigned his commission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200925\xe2\x80\x9327_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200925\xe2\x80\x9327-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> This expedition into the Ohio Country had international consequences. The French accused him of assassinating Jumonville, claiming that Jumonville was only on a diplomatic mission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200417\xe2\x80\x9318_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200417\xe2\x80\x9318-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Both France and Great Britain were set to fight for control of the region, both sending in troops in 1755, and war was formally declared in 1756.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2005100\xe2\x80\x9301_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2005100\xe2\x80\x9301-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg/220px-Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg/330px-Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg/440px-Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1167" data-file-height="826" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Destruction_de_l%27arm%C3%A9e_de_Braddock_en_1755.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Battle of Monogahela</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1755, the British Crown sent General <a href="/wiki/Edward_Braddock" title="Edward Braddock">Edward Braddock</a> and regulars to take Fort Duquesne.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> This was the largest British expedition to the colonies, intended to expel the French from the Ohio Country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden199335\xe2\x80\x9336_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199335\xe2\x80\x9336-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Braddock offered Washington the position of aide on his staff, and he accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Washington recommended that Braddock split the army into two divisions with a primary column and a second, more lightly equipped mobile offensive "flying column".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden199337Ferling201035\xe2\x80\x9336_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199337Ferling201035\xe2\x80\x9336-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> During the march, Washington became severely ill and was left behind, but he was able to rejoin Braddock at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Monongahela" title="Battle of the Monongahela">Monongahela</a>. The next day, the French and their Indian allies ambushed Braddock\'s divided forces, and Braddock was mortally wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden199337Ferling201035\xe2\x80\x9336_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199337Ferling201035\xe2\x80\x9336-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The British suffered devastating casualties and retreated in panic, with two-thirds killed or wounded, but Washington rallied his forces in an organized retreat even though he was suffering from a fever and headache.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden199337\xe2\x80\x9346_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199337\xe2\x80\x9346-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> He had two horses shot from under him, and his hat and coat were bullet-pierced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> His conduct under fire redeemed his reputation among critics of his command in the Battle of Fort Necessity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200928\xe2\x80\x9330_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200928\xe2\x80\x9330-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, he was not included by the succeeding commander Colonel Thomas Dunbar in planning subsequent operations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden199337\xe2\x80\x9346_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199337\xe2\x80\x9346-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On August 14, 1755, Dinwiddie appointed Washington colonel and commander in chief of all of Virginia\'s colonial military forces, to protect Virginia\'s frontier from Indian attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511\xe2\x80\x93512_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511\xe2\x80\x93512-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Washington was 23, in charge of defending 300 miles with only 300 men. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511\xe2\x80\x93512_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511\xe2\x80\x93512-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Savage frontier battles took place, 20 battles in 10 months between Washington\'s <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Regiment" title="Virginia Regiment">Virginia Regiment</a> and the Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511\xe2\x80\x93512Flexner1965138Fischer200415\xe2\x80\x9316Ellis200438_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511\xe2\x80\x93512Flexner1965138Fischer200415\xe2\x80\x9316Ellis200438-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Washington desired to wear the coveted red coat of officer rank, but this eluded him. He was convinced that Braddock would have recommended him for a regular commission in the British army had he survived, so he appealed to Braddock\'s successor <a href="/wiki/John_Campbell,_4th_Earl_of_Loudoun" title="John Campbell, 4th Earl of Loudoun">Lord Loudoun</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201072\xe2\x80\x9373_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201072\xe2\x80\x9373-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Loudon refused the request but did agree to transfer responsibility for Fort Cumberland from Virginia to Maryland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201072\xe2\x80\x9373_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201072\xe2\x80\x9373-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Washington\'s command increased to a thousand soldiers, while he emphasized discipline and training, and Virginia\'s frontier population suffered less than that of other colonies as a result of his strenuous efforts. Ellis concludes that "it was his only unqualified success" during the French and Indian War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer200415\xe2\x80\x9316Ellis200438_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer200415\xe2\x80\x9316Ellis200438-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FortDuquesne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/FortDuquesne.jpg/220px-FortDuquesne.jpg" width="220" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/FortDuquesne.jpg/330px-FortDuquesne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/FortDuquesne.jpg/440px-FortDuquesne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="673" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FortDuquesne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving depicting the British arrival at the remains of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Duquesne" title="Fort Duquesne">Fort Duquesne</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Washington continued to advocate the capture of Fort Duquesne,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and the British crown sent Commanding General <a href="/wiki/John_Forbes_(British_Army_officer)" title="John Forbes (British Army officer)">John Forbes</a>, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gage" title="Thomas Gage">Thomas Gage</a>, and British regulars to take the post in 1758.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Washington was promoted honorary Brigadier General, while he and two regiments under his command were ordered to cooperate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512Chernow201087_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512Chernow201087-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Expedition" title="Forbes Expedition">Forbes Expedition</a>, Washington suggested using an Indian-style warfare method, but this was ignored.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick193687Chernow2010512_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick193687Chernow2010512-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Washington was the only colonial officer among the British forces and was involved in only one battle during the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936512-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Forbes had devised a plan for an assault on Fort Duquesne and assigned Washington to lead one of three brigades. He was alerted to an enemy reconnaissance party in the area and sent Colonel <a href="/wiki/George_Mercer_(military_officer)" title="George Mercer (military officer)">George Mercer</a> and a contingent of several hundred Virginians to investigate. Gunshots were heard in the distance and Washington\'s unit responded, but they found themselves in a friendly-fire situation when reinforcements also arrived, each contingent thinking that the other was the French enemy. This incident resulted in minor casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201090_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201090-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Forbes assembled 2,500 men in late November for the final assault on the fort, and he promoted Washington to honorary brigadier general to head the operation. Washington and his army arrived on November 25 to find the fort abandoned and burned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200941\xe2\x80\x9342Chernow201090\xe2\x80\x9391_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200941\xe2\x80\x9342Chernow201090\xe2\x80\x9391-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The British did score a strategic victory by gaining control of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Ohio Valley">Ohio Valley</a>, but Washington retired from his Virginia Regiment commission in December 1758 and returned to Mount Vernon, while the French and Indian War was concluded in 1763 by the Treaty of Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELengel200575\xe2\x80\x9376,_81_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel200575\xe2\x80\x9376,_81-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Washington never gained the Royal commission which he sought, but he acquired military, political, and leadership skills which proved invaluable during the American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201091\xe2\x80\x9393Lengel200580_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201091\xe2\x80\x9393Lengel200580-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Historians ascribe his support of a strong national government and a vigorous executive to his frustrations with officials in these and later interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201092\xe2\x80\x9393_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201092\xe2\x80\x9393-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2004218Ferling200932\xe2\x80\x9333,_200,_258\xe2\x80\x93272,_316_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004218Ferling200932\xe2\x80\x9333,_200,_258\xe2\x80\x93272,_316-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> He developed a distinct preference for regular troops over undisciplined militia, even though his command was limited during this war to smaller and more rural forces than during the Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHigginbotham198522\xe2\x80\x9325_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHigginbotham198522\xe2\x80\x9325-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman1968136\xe2\x80\x9337_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman1968136\xe2\x80\x9337-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_the_Enlightenment">Religion and the Enlightenment</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_and_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="George Washington and religion">George Washington and religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Enlightenment" title="American Enlightenment">American Enlightenment</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a></div>\n<p>Washington\'s religious views were complex. He was descended from Anglican minister <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Washington_(1602%E2%80%931653)" title="Lawrence Washington (1602\xe2\x80\x931653)">Lawrence Washington</a>, whose troubles with the Church of England may have caused his heirs to emigrate to America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETsakiridis2018_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETsakiridis2018-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> He was a devoted member of the Anglican Church and was baptized as an infant in April 1732.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow20106Morrison2009136Alden19932,_26Randall199717Tsakiridis2018_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20106Morrison2009136Alden19932,_26Randall199717Tsakiridis2018-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>  He served as a vestryman and as church warden for Fairfax Parish in Alexandria and Truro Parish, posts that he maintained for 22 years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010130Thompson200840Tsakiridis2018_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010130Thompson200840Tsakiridis2018-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> He privately prayed  and read the Bible daily, and he publicly encouraged people and the nation to pray.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrazer2012198\xe2\x80\x93199Chernow2010119,_132Tsakiridis2018_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazer2012198\xe2\x80\x93199Chernow2010119,_132Tsakiridis2018-85">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> He may have taken communion on a regular basis prior to the Revolutionary War, but he did not do so following the war, and Pastor <a href="/wiki/James_Abercrombie_(Episcopal_priest)" title="James Abercrombie (Episcopal priest)">James Abercrombie</a> admonished him for not setting an example.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131,_470Johnson191987\xe2\x80\x93195Espinosa200952Frazer2012201\xe2\x80\x93203Tsakiridis2018_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131,_470Johnson191987\xe2\x80\x93195Espinosa200952Frazer2012201\xe2\x80\x93203Tsakiridis2018-86">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Masonic_print.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Washington_Masonic_print.jpg/170px-Washington_Masonic_print.jpg" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Washington_Masonic_print.jpg/255px-Washington_Masonic_print.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Washington_Masonic_print.jpg/340px-Washington_Masonic_print.jpg 2x" data-file-width="936" data-file-height="1414" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Masonic_print.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>George Washington as Master of his Lodge, 1793</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington believed in a "wise, inscrutable, and irresistible" Creator God who was active in the Universe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETsakiridis2018_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETsakiridis2018-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> He referred to this God by the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> terms <i>Providence</i>, <i>The Creator</i>, or <i>The Almighty</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall199767Tsakiridis2018_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199767Tsakiridis2018-87">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> He believed in a divine power who watched over battlefields, was involved in the outcome of war, was protecting his life, and was involved in American politics\xe2\x80\x94specifically the creation of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131Tsakiridis2018_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131Tsakiridis2018-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Historian Ron Chernow suggests that Washington avoided evangelistic Christianity or hellfire and brimstone speech, as well as communion or anything that would "flaunt his religiosity". Chernow points out that he "never used his religion as a device for partisan purposes or in official undertakings".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131\xe2\x80\x93132_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131\xe2\x80\x93132-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> No mention of Jesus Christ appears in his <a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Papers" title="The Washington Papers">private correspondence</a>, and such references are rare in his public writings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENovak200795Tsakiridis2018_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENovak200795Tsakiridis2018-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> He often quoted from the Bible or paraphrased it, and often referred to the Anglican Book of Common Prayer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131\xe2\x80\x93132Morrison2009136Tsakiridis2018_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131\xe2\x80\x93132Morrison2009136Tsakiridis2018-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> There is debate on whether he is best classed as a Christian, a theistic rationalist, or both.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrazer2012197\xe2\x80\x93198,_201\xe2\x80\x93203Novak2007158\xe2\x80\x93161_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazer2012197\xe2\x80\x93198,_201\xe2\x80\x93203Novak2007158\xe2\x80\x93161-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington emphasized religious toleration in a nation with numerous denominations and religions; when referring to God, he would use terms such as, <i>Creator, Divine Author</i>, and <i>Supreme Being</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENovak2007122_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENovak2007122-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> He attended services of different Christian denominations and suppressed anti-Catholic celebrations in the Army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoller1963125_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoller1963125-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> He permitted Jews, Muslims, Christians of any denomination, and atheists to work at Mount Vernon. While President, he saluted 22 major religious sects and gave speeches on religious toleration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> He was deeply rooted in the ideas, values, and modes of thinking of the Enlightenment, more so than his contemporaries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood2001313_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2001313-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> He harbored no contempt of organized Christianity and its clergy, "being no bigot myself to any mode of worship".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood2001313_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2001313-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> He proclaimed in 1793, "We have abundant reason to rejoice that in this Land the light of truth and reason has triumphed over the power of bigotry and superstition."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELillbackNewcombe2006313\xe2\x80\x93314_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELillbackNewcombe2006313\xe2\x80\x93314-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington became a member of the Freemasons, more commonly known as the Masonic Lodge. It was a socially acceptable institution in the late 18th century which advocated moral teachings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010132,_500Morrison2009136Stavish2007XIX,_XXIImmekus2018_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010132,_500Morrison2009136Stavish2007XIX,_XXIImmekus2018-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>  He was attracted by the Masons\' dedication to the Enlightenment principles of rationality, reason, and brotherhood. The American lodges did not share the anti-clerical perspective of the controversial European lodges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201027,_704_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201027,_704-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> A Masonic lodge was established in <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg, Virginia</a> in September 1752 and he was initiated two months later as one of its first Entered Apprentices. Within a year, he progressed through its ranks to become a Master Mason.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall199767Chernow201027_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199767Chernow201027-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Before and during the American Revolution, he used Masonic lodges as meeting places to plot against the British.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmekus2018_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmekus2018-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> He had a high regard for the Masonic Order, but his personal lodge attendance was sporadic. In 1777, a convention of Virginia lodges asked him to be the Grand Master of the newly established <a href="/wiki/Grand_Lodge_of_Virginia" title="Grand Lodge of Virginia">Grand Lodge of Virginia</a>, but he declined due to his commitment leading the Continental Army. After 1782, however, he corresponded frequently with Masonic lodges and members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEImmekus2018_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEImmekus2018-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In 1788, he was listed as Master in the Virginia charter of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria-Washington_Lodge_No._22" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexandria-Washington Lodge No. 22">Alexandria Lodge No. 22</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;A_Brief_History&quot;_(GWMNMA)_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;A_Brief_History&quot;_(GWMNMA)-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Marriage_and_civilian_life_.281759.E2.80.931774.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_civilian_life_(1759\xe2\x80\x931774)">Marriage and civilian life (1759\xe2\x80\x931774)</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_1772.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Washington_1772.jpg/170px-Washington_1772.jpg" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Washington_1772.jpg/255px-Washington_1772.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Washington_1772.jpg/340px-Washington_1772.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2418" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_1772.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colonel George Washington, Provincial Troops, Virginia Regiment, by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale" title="Charles Willson Peale">Charles Willson Peale</a>, 1772</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington married <a href="/wiki/Martha_Washington" title="Martha Washington">Martha Dandridge Custis</a> on January 6, 1759, the wealthy widow of <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Parke_Custis" title="Daniel Parke Custis">Daniel Parke Custis</a>. He was 27 and she was 28, and the ceremony took place at the Custis mansion. Martha was intelligent, gracious, and experienced in managing a planter\'s estate, and they made a harmonious marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200233\xe2\x80\x9334Wiencek200369_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200233\xe2\x80\x9334Wiencek200369-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> They raised <a href="/wiki/John_Parke_Custis" title="John Parke Custis">John Parke Custis</a> and Martha Parke (Patsy) Custis, her children from her previous marriage, and they later raised their grandchildren <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Parke_Custis_Lewis" title="Eleanor Parke Custis Lewis">Eleanor Parke Custis</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Parke_Custis" title="George Washington Parke Custis">George Washington Parke Custis</a>. They had no children together; his 1751 bout with smallpox may have made him sterile, and he grieved privately over not having his own children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010103Flexner197442\xe2\x80\x9343Bumgarner19941\xe2\x80\x938_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010103Flexner197442\xe2\x80\x9343Bumgarner19941\xe2\x80\x938-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> They moved to Mount Vernon near Alexandria where he took up life as a successful planter of tobacco and wheat and emerged as a political figure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201097\xe2\x80\x9398Fischer200414_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201097\xe2\x80\x9398Fischer200414-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\xe2\x80\x99s marriage also made him one of Virginia\'s wealthiest men and increased his social standing. He acquired control over Martha\xe2\x80\x99s one-third dower interest in the 18,000-acre (73&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Parke_Custis#Estate" title="Daniel Parke Custis">Custis estate</a>, worth approximately $100,000, and he managed the remaining two-thirds on behalf of Martha\'s children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiencek200367\xe2\x80\x9369,_336_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek200367\xe2\x80\x9369,_336-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> He also acquired 84 slaves through the marriage, brought to Mount Vernon from the estate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_Washington_&amp;_Slavery&quot;_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_Washington_&amp;_Slavery&quot;-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dinwiddie had promised land bounties in 1754 to the soldiers and officers who volunteered during the French and Indian War;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999100_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999100-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Washington prevailed upon Governor <a href="/wiki/Norborne_Berkeley,_4th_Baron_Botetourt" title="Norborne Berkeley, 4th Baron Botetourt">Lord Botetourt</a>, and he fulfilled Dinwiddie\'s promise in 1769\xe2\x80\x9370.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010184_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010184-109">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Washington received title to 23,200 acres (94&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) where the <a href="/wiki/Kanawha_River" title="Kanawha River">Kanawha River</a> flows into the Ohio River in West Virginia;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002135\xe2\x80\x9337_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002135\xe2\x80\x9337-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> he also bought additional land, doubled the size of Mount Vernon to 6,500 acres (26&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), and increased its slave population to more than 100 by 1775.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200441\xe2\x80\x9342,_48_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200441\xe2\x80\x9342,_48-111">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a respected military hero and landowner, he held local office and was elected to the Virginia provincial legislature, representing Frederick County in the House of Burgesses for seven years beginning in 1758.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200441\xe2\x80\x9342,_48_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200441\xe2\x80\x9342,_48-111">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In the election that year, he plied the voters with 170 US gallons (640&#160;l) of rice punch, beer, wine, hard cider, and brandy while he was away serving on the Forbes Expedition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden199371_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199371-112">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> He won election with roughly 40 percent of the vote, defeating three other candidates with the help of several local elites. He rarely spoke publicly in his early legislative career, but he became a prominent critic of Britain\'s taxation and mercantilist policies in the 1760s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200949\xe2\x80\x9354,_68_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200949\xe2\x80\x9354,_68-113">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg/170px-Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg" width="170" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg/255px-Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="289" data-file-height="412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martha_Dandridge_Custis_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mezzotint of Martha Washington based on a 1757 portrait by <a href="/wiki/John_Wollaston_(painter)" title="John Wollaston (painter)">John Wollaston</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Washington lived an aristocratic lifestyle, and his favorite activities included fox hunting, fishing, dances and parties, the theater, races, and cockfights. He also was known to play cards, backgammon, and billiards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200243\xe2\x80\x9344Ellis200444_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200243\xe2\x80\x9344Ellis200444-114">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Like most Virginia planters, he imported luxuries and other goods from England and paid for them by exporting his tobacco crop. By 1764, however, a poor tobacco market left him \xe2\x82\xa41,800 in debt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200449\xe2\x80\x9350_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200449\xe2\x80\x9350-115">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> He bolstered his solvency in the mid-1760s by diversifying, paying more attention to his finances, and reducing imported luxuries. He changed Mount Vernon\'s primary cash crop from tobacco to wheat, and he further diversified operations to include <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Gristmill" title="George Washington&#39;s Gristmill">flour milling</a>, fishing, horse breeding, hog production, spinning, and weaving.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPogue20042\xe2\x80\x9310_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPogue20042\xe2\x80\x9310-116">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In the 1790s, he erected a <a href="/wiki/File:George_Washington%27s_Distillery.JPG" title="File:George Washington&#39;s Distillery.JPG">distillery</a> for whiskey production which yielded more than 1,000 US gallons (3,800&#160;l) a month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld199744\xe2\x80\x9345Ferling2009351_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld199744\xe2\x80\x9345Ferling2009351-117">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the fall of 1770, Washington inspected the bounty lands in the Ohio and Great Kanawha regions, promised to French and Indian War veterans. He secured the appointment of <a href="/wiki/William_Crawford_(soldier)" title="William Crawford (soldier)">William Crawford</a> to make a survey of the lands, who gave Washington the best acreage on the tract. Washington told the veterans that their land was hilly and unsuitable for farming, and agreed to purchase 20,147 acres; many veterans were happy with the sale, while others felt they had been duped.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200244\xe2\x80\x9345_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200244\xe2\x80\x9345-118">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s step-daughter Patsy Custis died in his arms on June 19, 1773 after suffering from epileptic attacks for five years. The following day, he wrote to <a href="/wiki/Burwell_Bassett" title="Burwell Bassett">Burwell Bassett</a>: "It is easier to conceive, than to describe, the distress of this Family\xe2\x80\xa6."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010161_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010161-119">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> He cancelled all business activity and was not away from Martha for a single night for the next three months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHigginbotham2001154_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHigginbotham2001154-120">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Patsy\'s death enabled him to pay off his British creditors, however, since half of her inheritance passed to him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling201066\xe2\x80\x9367Ellis200450\xe2\x80\x9353Higginbotham200167\xe2\x80\x9393_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling201066\xe2\x80\x9367Ellis200450\xe2\x80\x9353Higginbotham200167\xe2\x80\x9393-121">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington became a political figure and soon emerged as a leader among the social elite in Virginia. From 1768 to 1775, he invited some 2,000 guests to his Mount Vernon estate, mostly those whom he considered "people of rank". His advice regarding people who were not of high social status was to "treat them civilly" but "keep them at a proper distance, for they will grow upon familiarity, in proportion as you sink in authority".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer200414_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer200414-122">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> He became more politically active in 1769, presenting legislation in the Virginia Assembly to establish an embargo on goods from Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200273\xe2\x80\x9376_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200273\xe2\x80\x9376-123">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Revolution">American Revolution</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_in_the_American_Revolution" title="George Washington in the American Revolution">George Washington in the American Revolution</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III,_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg/220px-Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg/330px-Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg/440px-Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III%2C_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1597" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel_Pulling_Down_the_Statue_of_King_George_III,_N.Y.C._ca._1859.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Pulling Down the Statue of King George III, N.Y.C.</i>, July 9, 1776, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Adam_Simon_Oertel" title="Johannes Adam Simon Oertel">Johannes Adam Simon Oertel</a> (1859)</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington played a leading political and military role in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. His sense of diffidence towards England began when he was passed over for a commission in the British Army in 1755.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010136_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010136-124">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> He and other colonists were angered by a Royal Proclamation in 1763 which banned American settlement west of the <a href="/wiki/Alleghany_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Alleghany Mountains">Alleghany Mountains</a>, in order to protect the British fur trade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010137Taylor201661_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010137Taylor201661-125">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> He believed that the <a href="/wiki/Stamp_Act_1765" title="Stamp Act 1765">Stamp Act</a> of 1765 was an "Act of Oppression", and he celebrated with his fellow colonists after its appeal the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200968_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200968-128">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup> In March 1766, Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Declaratory_Act" title="Declaratory Act">Declaratory Act</a> which asserted that Parliamentary law held absolute sway over colonial law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016103_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016103-131">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Washington helped to lead the widespread colonial protests against the <a href="/wiki/Townshend_Acts" title="Townshend Acts">Townshend Acts</a> passed by Parliament in 1767, and he introduced a proposal in May 1769 drafted by <a href="/wiki/George_Mason" title="George Mason">George Mason</a> which called for Virginia to boycott English goods until the Acts were repealed. Parliament repealed them in 1770.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman1968174\xe2\x80\x9376Taylor201675_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman1968174\xe2\x80\x9376Taylor201675-132">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parliament instituted the <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a> in 1774 as punitive measures for the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party" title="Boston Tea Party">Boston Tea Party</a>, and Washington referred to them as "an Invasion of our Rights and Privileges".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997262Chernow2010166_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997262Chernow2010166-133">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> He said that Americans must not submit to acts of tyranny since "custom and use shall make us as tame and abject slaves, as the blacks we rule over with such arbitrary sway".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993101_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993101-134">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> In July 1774, George Mason arrived at Mount Vernon with a list of resolutions which he and Washington worked overnight to refine. The next day, they presented them to the Fairfax County committee, chaired by Washington, which adopted the <a href="/wiki/Fairfax_Resolves" title="Fairfax Resolves">Fairfax Resolves</a> calling for a Continental Congress, among other things.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010167_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010167-135">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In August, he attended the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Conventions" title="Virginia Conventions">First Virginia Convention</a> where he was selected as a delegate to the <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">First Continental Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2010100FordHuntFitzpatrick1904V19:_11_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2010100FordHuntFitzpatrick1904V19:_11-136">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> On July 4, 1776, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> from Britain. Patriots openly attacked symbols of monarchy and toppled an equestrian statue of <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a> in New York City five days later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016160\xe2\x80\x93161_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016160\xe2\x80\x93161-137">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Revolutionary_War_.281775.E2.80.931783.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_War_(1775\xe2\x80\x931783)">Revolutionary War (1775\xe2\x80\x931783)</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_career_of_George_Washington" title="Military career of George Washington">Military career of George Washington</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Washington,_1776.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg/220px-George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg" width="220" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg/330px-George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg/440px-George_Washington%2C_1776.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1319" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Washington,_1776.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>General George Washington,</center>commander of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Revolutionary War with Great Britain began April 19, 1775 at the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord" title="Battles of Lexington and Concord">Battles of Lexington and Concord</a>, while the Continentals besieged the British in Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016132\xe2\x80\x93133_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016132\xe2\x80\x93133-138">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> in Philadelphia officially assumed command of the troops in Boston on June 14 and created the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>. <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Adams" title="Samuel Adams">Samuel Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> passed over <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock" title="John Hancock">John Hancock</a> to nominate Washington as supreme commander, and he was unanimously elected the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200467\xe2\x80\x9368Chernow2010185\xe2\x80\x93186Fitzpatrick1936514_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200467\xe2\x80\x9368Chernow2010185\xe2\x80\x93186Fitzpatrick1936514-139">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington appeared at the Congress, poised for war in a military uniform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999294Fitzpatrick1936514Taylor2016141\xe2\x80\x93142_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999294Fitzpatrick1936514Taylor2016141\xe2\x80\x93142-140">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> He declined a salary in his acceptance speech but received reimbursement of expenses for which he fully accounted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200986\xe2\x80\x9387Fitzpatrick1936514_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200986\xe2\x80\x9387Fitzpatrick1936514-141">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The Patriot Continental Congress chose capable subordinate officers to aid him, including Major General <a href="/wiki/Artemas_Ward" title="Artemas Ward">Artemas Ward</a>, Adjutant General <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Gates" title="Horatio Gates">Horatio Gates</a>, Major General <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lee_(general)" title="Charles Lee (general)">Charles Lee</a>, Major General <a href="/wiki/Philip_Schuyler" title="Philip Schuyler">Philip Schuyler</a>, Major General <a href="/wiki/Nathanael_Greene" title="Nathanael Greene">Nathanael Greene</a>, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Henry_Knox" title="Henry Knox">Henry Knox</a>, and Colonel <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010190\xe2\x80\x93191Ferling2002108_142-0" class="reference"><a
