href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010190\xe2\x80\x93191Ferling2002108-142">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Washington was impressed by the enthusiasm of Colonel <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a> and gave him the responsibilities of invading Canada. He also engaged Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Morgan" title="Daniel Morgan">Daniel Morgan</a> with whom he had served in the French and Indian War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002109\xe2\x80\x93110_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002109\xe2\x80\x93110-143">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 2, 1775, Washington inspected his new army at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a> and was astonished to find a ragtag assembly of undisciplined, poorly sheltered, and badly outfitted soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016143_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016143-144">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> He formally took command on July 3 and immediately initiated reforms. He drilled soldiers and imposed strict discipline, including fines, floggings, and incarceration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002112Taylor2016143Fitzpatrick1936514_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002112Taylor2016143Fitzpatrick1936514-145">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> He ordered his officer staff to familiarize themselves with their recruits so as to understand the military duties best suited to them, to respect civilians, and to read military manuals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002112\xe2\x80\x93113_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002112\xe2\x80\x93113-146">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> He removed cowardly or incompetent officers, and he told Congress that it was a "most necessary Work".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002116_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002116-147">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> On August 23, King <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a> proclaimed that rebellious American colonists were traitors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016144_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016144-148">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Quebec.2C_Boston.2C_and_Long_Island"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Quebec,_Boston,_and_Long_Island">Quebec, Boston, and Long Island</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Quebec_(1775)" title="Invasion of Quebec (1775)">Invasion of Quebec (1775)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Boston" title="Siege of Boston">Siege of Boston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island" title="Battle of Long Island">Battle of Long Island</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg/170px-WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg" width="170" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg/255px-WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg/340px-WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1054" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WashingtonAtDorchesterHeightsByStuart.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Washington at Dorchester Heights</i>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a> (1806)</div></div></div>\n<p>In September 1775, Washington sent staff officer Arnold and 1,000 troops to Canada to aid General <a href="/wiki/Richard_Montgomery" title="Richard Montgomery">Richard Montgomery</a>\'s siege of British-held Quebec and to secure the northern flank.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016151\xe2\x80\x93152_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016151\xe2\x80\x93152-149">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Quebec was reinforced by 7,000 British troops and the American siege collapsed, forcing the Continental Army to make a hasty retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016153_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016153-150">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Later that month, an impatient Washington called a council of war and proposed an attack on the besieged British Army in Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002117_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002117-151">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> However, his generals declined the ambitious plan, concerned about high casualties on an entrenched enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002117\xe2\x80\x93118_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002117\xe2\x80\x93118-152">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington started afresh with new recruits in January 1776, since soldiers left the Army after their enlistments expired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016144_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016144-148">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> By mid-January, his army was half-strength at 9,600 men, and the colonial militia who fought in the French and Indian War were summoned to fill in the gaps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002116\xe2\x80\x93117_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002116\xe2\x80\x93117-153">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup> The new British commander at Boston was General <a href="/wiki/William_Howe,_5th_Viscount_Howe" title="William Howe, 5th Viscount Howe">William Howe</a>, but he did not attack during this time\xe2\x80\x94which was probably fortunate for Washington and the burgeoning Continental Army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002117_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002117-151">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late 1775, Washington sent staff officer Henry Knox to the recently <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Fort_Ticonderoga" title="Capture of Fort Ticonderoga">captured Fort Ticonderoga</a> for gunpowder and cannons. He <a href="/wiki/Noble_train_of_artillery" title="Noble train of artillery">transported them</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fortification_of_Dorchester_Heights" title="Fortification of Dorchester Heights">Dorchester Heights</a> in February where he was joined by Washington, who used them to drive the British out of Boston. General Howe immediately <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_Day_(Massachusetts)" title="Evacuation Day (Massachusetts)">evacuated Boston</a> with 10,000 troops and 1,100 <a href="/wiki/Loyalism" title="Loyalism">loyalists</a>. Washington then marched his Army to New York, initiated fortification, and correctly predicted that the British would return and attack in full force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELengel2005124\xe2\x80\x93126Higginbotham1985125\xe2\x80\x9334Ferling2002118\xe2\x80\x93119Taylor2016153\xe2\x80\x93154Fitzpatrick1936514_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2005124\xe2\x80\x93126Higginbotham1985125\xe2\x80\x9334Ferling2002118\xe2\x80\x93119Taylor2016153\xe2\x80\x93154Fitzpatrick1936514-156">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Howe resupplied in <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a> and headed for New York City with the British fleet, as it was considered the key to securing the continent. The British forces assembled in <a href="/wiki/New_York_Bay" title="New York Bay">New York Bay</a>, including more than 100 ships and thousands of troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936514\xe2\x80\x93515_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936514\xe2\x80\x93515-157">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Howe\'s army landed unopposed on <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">Staten Island</a> on July 2, and British ships continued to arrive from England and Carolina for a siege of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016162\xe2\x80\x93163_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016162\xe2\x80\x93163-158">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>  Howe\'s troop strength totaled 32,000 well-trained soldiers, including 8,000 <a href="/wiki/Hessian_(soldier)" title="Hessian (soldier)">Hessians</a>, while Washington\'s troop strength consisted of 23,000 soldiers, 19,000 of whom were raw recruits and militia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010244\xe2\x80\x93245Taylor2016162\xe2\x80\x93163_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010244\xe2\x80\x93245Taylor2016162\xe2\x80\x93163-159">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>  On August 22, Howe landed 20,000 troops at <a href="/wiki/Gravesend,_Brooklyn" title="Gravesend, Brooklyn">Gravesend, Brooklyn</a> and approached Washington\'s fortifications. Washington chose to fight despite the objections of his generals,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200495\xe2\x80\x9396_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200495\xe2\x80\x9396-160">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> as he had received false information that Howe\'s army had only 8,000 to 9,000 troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010244_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010244-161">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Howe <a href="/wiki/New_York_and_New_Jersey_campaign" title="New York and New Jersey campaign">assaulted Washington\'s flank</a> on August 27 and inflicted 1,500 Patriot casualties, while the British suffered 400 casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016164_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016164-162">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>  Washington and his generals decided to retreat, and he instructed General <a href="/wiki/William_Heath" title="William Heath">William Heath</a> to make available every flat-bottomed riverboat and sloop in the area. General <a href="/wiki/William_Alexander,_Lord_Stirling" title="William Alexander, Lord Stirling">William Alexander</a> held off the British Army and covered the retreat, and the army safely crossed the <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">East River</a> under the cover of darkness to <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Manhattan Island">Manhattan Island</a> without loss of life or material\xe2\x80\x94although the British captured General Alexander.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2005186\xe2\x80\x9395_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2005186\xe2\x80\x9395-163">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battleoflongisland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Battleoflongisland.jpg/220px-Battleoflongisland.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Battleoflongisland.jpg/330px-Battleoflongisland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Battleoflongisland.jpg/440px-Battleoflongisland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="622" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battleoflongisland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Battle of Long Island</i><br /><a href="/wiki/Alonzo_Chappel" title="Alonzo Chappel">Alonzo Chappel</a> (1858)</div></div></div>\n<p>Howe was emboldened by his victory at Long Island and sent a dispatch addressed to "George Washington, Esq." attempting to negotiate peace. Washington declined the overture and demanded that he be addressed as a General and recognized as a fellow belligerent, not as a "rebel". He was concerned that his men would be hanged as rebels if they were captured, and he believed it his duty to insist that his men and the newly established United States be recognized with proper diplomatic protocol. The attempts at negotiation failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010240Davis197593\xe2\x80\x9394Taylor2016164_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010240Davis197593\xe2\x80\x9394Taylor2016164-164">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British navy bombarded unstable earthworks on lower Manhattan Island that had been constructed by the Patriots.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016165_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016165-165">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Washington considered abandoning the island, including <a href="/wiki/Fort_Washington_(New_York)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort Washington (New York)">Fort Washington</a>, but he heeded the advice of Generals Greene and <a href="/wiki/Israel_Putnam" title="Israel Putnam">Israel Putnam</a> to defend the fort. They were unable to hold it, however, and Washington abandoned it despite General Lee\'s objections; his army retired north to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_White_Plains" title="Battle of White Plains">White Plains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis1975136Chernow2010257_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis1975136Chernow2010257-166">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Howe pursued, and Washington was forced to retreat across the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lee_Historic_Park" title="Fort Lee Historic Park">Fort Lee</a> and avoid encirclement. Howe took the offensive; he landed his troops on the island on November 16, surrounded and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Washington" title="Battle of Fort Washington">captured Fort Washington</a>, and inflicted high casualties on the Americans. Washington was responsible for the decision to delay the retreat, but he also faulted the Congress and Nathaniel Greene. Loyalists in New York considered Howe a liberator and spread a rumor that Washington had set fire to the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993137Taylor2016165_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993137Taylor2016165-167">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The morale in the Patriot army was at its lowest ebb, as the British captured General Lee while he was with his mistress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166\xe2\x80\x93167_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166\xe2\x80\x93167-168">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Delaware_crossing">Delaware crossing</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_crossing_of_the_Delaware_River" title="George Washington&#39;s crossing of the Delaware River">George Washington\'s crossing of the Delaware River</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Emanuel_Leutze_(American,_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington,_D.C.)_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Emanuel_Leutze_%28American%2C_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington%2C_D.C.%29_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/260px-Emanuel_Leutze_%28American%2C_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington%2C_D.C.%29_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="260" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Emanuel_Leutze_%28American%2C_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington%2C_D.C.%29_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/390px-Emanuel_Leutze_%28American%2C_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington%2C_D.C.%29_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Emanuel_Leutze_%28American%2C_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington%2C_D.C.%29_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/520px-Emanuel_Leutze_%28American%2C_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington%2C_D.C.%29_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3874" data-file-height="2269" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Emanuel_Leutze_(American,_Schw%C3%A4bisch_Gm%C3%BCnd_1816%E2%80%931868_Washington,_D.C.)_-_Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware" title="Washington Crossing the Delaware">Washington Crossing the Delaware</a>, December 25, 1776</i>, Emanuel Leutze (1851)<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Washington\'s Patriot army retreated through northeast New Jersey, reduced to 5,400 troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166-171">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Howe broke off pursuit on December 14, delayed his advance on Philadelphia, and set up winter quarters in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004224\xe2\x80\x93226Taylor2016169_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004224\xe2\x80\x93226Taylor2016169-172">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Washington crossed the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a> into Pennsylvania, where Lee\'s replacement <a href="/wiki/John_Sullivan_(general)" title="John Sullivan (general)">John Sullivan</a> and 2,000 troops joined him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166\xe2\x80\x93167,_169_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166\xe2\x80\x93167,_169-173">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The future of the Continental Army was in doubt due to lack of basic supplies, expiring enlistments, a harsh winter and desertions. Washington was disappointed to discover that many New Jersey residents were  Loyalists, or simply skeptical about the prospect of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999235Chernow2010264_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999235Chernow2010264-174">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Howe had split up his British Army and posted a Hessian garrison at <a href="/wiki/Trenton,_New_Jersey" title="Trenton, New Jersey">Trenton, New Jersey</a> to hold western New Jersey and the east shore of the Delaware.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016169_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016169-175">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Learning of the complacency of the Howe\'s British Army, Washington met with his generals on Christmas Eve and devised an intricate plan of a surprise attack on the Hessian garrison at Trenton.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270,272_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270,272-176">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Washington believed the future of the country was riding on his shoulders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273-177">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> His code name for the campaign was "<i>Victory or Death</i>". Washington divided his army to cross the Delaware at three places, led by Washington (<i>2,400 troops</i>), General <a href="/wiki/James_Ewing_(Pennsylvania)" title="James Ewing (Pennsylvania)">James Ewing</a> (<i>700 troops</i>), and Colonel <a href="/wiki/John_Cadwalader_(general)" title="John Cadwalader (general)">John Cadwalader</a> (<i>1,500 troops</i>), to reach the Hessians at Trenton. Washington\'s force would then be split, with Washington taking the Pennington Road, and General Sullivan traveling on the river\'s edge, traveling south. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010272_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010272-178">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the crossing, Washington ordered a 60-mile search for barges along the Delaware to transport his army, particularly <a href="/wiki/Durham_boat" title="Durham boat">Durham boats</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup> and ordered the destruction of any vessels that could be used by the British.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270\xe2\x80\x93272Randall1997319_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270\xe2\x80\x93272Randall1997319-181">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>On Christmas Day, at sunset, Washington crossed the Delaware and staked out the river on the Jersey shore, at risk of being captured. The weather worsened by a winter storm, turning to heavy sleet, hail, and snow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273-177">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Washington\'s men crossed at McKonkey\'s Ferry, crowded on Durham boats, up to 40 men per vessel, obstructed by river ice. A northeaster wind churned up the waters, and Washington\'s men were pelted by hail exposed in their boats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273_177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010273-177">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Washington\'s troops finally maded it all across, without losing a man, at 3:00 A.M. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Knox" title="Henry Knox">Henry Knox</a>, in charge of transporting artillery, was delayed due to problems with managing frightened horses and transporting 18 field guns on flat bottom ferries. Cadwalader and Ewing, however, failed to cross the Delaware because of ice and heavy currents. Washington waited in dispair, while Knox and the rest of the Army crossed the Delaware, and feared he might have to abandon his attack on Trenton.  <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004171Taylor2016215\xe2\x80\x93219_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004171Taylor2016215\xe2\x80\x93219-182">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> By 4:00 AM, Knox and his artillery regiments, finally made it across the Delaware. Rather than return his army to Pennsylvania, at risk of being spotted, Washington chose to go alone against the Hessians.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trenton_campaign">Trenton campaign</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton" title="Battle of Princeton">Battle of Princeton</a></div>\n<p>Washington\'s scouts reported the location of the Hessian forward positions about one mile from Trenton. Washington split his force into two columns and rode about giving words of encouragement to his men: "Soldiers keep by your officers. For God\'s sake, keep by your officers." Washington stopped briefly at a nearby home while his senior officers gathered for a council of war. Moving on, the two columns separated at the Birmingham crossroads, with General <a href="/wiki/Nathanael_Greene" title="Nathanael Greene">Nathanael Greene</a>\'s force taking the upper Ferry Road, led by Washington, while General <a href="/wiki/John_Sullivan_(general)" title="John Sullivan (general)">John Sullivan\'s</a> advanced on River Road.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004228\xe2\x80\x93230_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004228\xe2\x80\x93230-183">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Trenton.Dean.USMA.edu.history.gif" title="File:Battle of Trenton.Dean.USMA.edu.history.gif">See map</a>) Veiled by stormy weather, the Americans advanced on Trenton through the snow, sleet, and hail. Many of Washington\'s soldiers had no shoes and their feet were bloodied, while two soldiers died of exposure. At sunrise, in coordinated effort, the army attacked the unsuspecting Hessians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2000146-147Mays2010336_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2000146-147Mays2010336-184">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Washington personally led the charge, aided by Major-General <a href="/wiki/Henry_Knox" title="Henry Knox">Henry Knox</a> and his artillery,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004232\xe2\x80\x93234,_405_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004232\xe2\x80\x93234,_405-185">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> with Hessian Colonel <a href="/wiki/Johann_Rall" title="Johann Rall">Johann Rall</a> mortally wounded during the short battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010276_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010276-186">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg/220px-The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg/330px-The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg/440px-The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1992" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Capture of the Hessians at Trenton, December 26, 1776</i><br />John Trumbull</div></div></div>\n<p>Hessian casualties included 22 killed 83 wounded. Washington also captured 850 British and Hessian troops and large amounts of supply and retreated back across the Delaware to Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004254_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004254-187">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> On January 3, he returned to New Jersey and launched an attack on British regulars at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton" title="Battle of Princeton">Princeton</a>, with only 40 Americans killed or wounded while the British forces suffered 273 killed or captured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999306\xe2\x80\x93307Alden1993146_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999306\xe2\x80\x93307Alden1993146-188">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> American Generals <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Mercer" title="Hugh Mercer">Hugh Mercer</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Cadwalader_(general)" title="John Cadwalader (general)">John Cadwalader</a> were already present and being driven back by the British, then Mercer was mortally wounded. Washington arrived at the scene and rallied the men to counterattack, himself out front and advancing to within 30 yards of the British line.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993145_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993145-189">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The remaining British troops retreated after making a brief stand, some evacuating Princeton and others taking refuge in <a href="/wiki/Nassau_Hall" title="Nassau Hall">Nassau Hall</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> brought three cannons and began firing at the building where the British were holed up. Washington\'s troops charged, and in less than an hour the British put out the white flag of surrender; 194 soldiers walked out of the building and laid down their arms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999361\xe2\x80\x93364Fischer2004339Chernow2010276\xe2\x80\x93278_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999361\xe2\x80\x93364Fischer2004339Chernow2010276\xe2\x80\x93278-190">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Howe retreated to New York City and its environs, where his army remained inactive until Spring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172-191">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Washington\'s depleted Continental Army took up winter headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Morristown,_New_Jersey" title="Morristown, New Jersey">Morristown, New Jersey</a>, allowing them to disrupt British supply lines and drive the British from parts of New Jersey. He later admitted that the British could easily have defeated his thinly guarded encampment if they had counter-attacked before his troops were dug in.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010285\xe2\x80\x93286_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010285\xe2\x80\x93286-192">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s victories were pivotal for the revolution and wrecked the British strategy of showing overwhelming force, then offering generous terms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004367_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004367-193">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the British still controlled New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172-191">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> After the winter campaign, however, many Patriot soldiers did not re-enlist or had deserted during the harsh winter. Washington and Congress responded with increased rewards for re-enlisting and punishment for desertion, which effected greater troop numbers for later battles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004151_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004151-194">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In the last two weeks of February, 1777, word reached London of Washington\'s victories at Trenton and Princeton, bringing with it the realization that the Americans were now in a position to demand complete independence with no conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2007188_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2007188-195">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Brandywine.2C_Germantown.2C_and_Saratoga"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Brandywine,_Germantown,_and_Saratoga">Brandywine, Germantown, and Saratoga</span></h3>\n<p>The Americans were encamped at Morristown, New Jersey in February 1777, and Washington became convinced that smallpox inoculation was required to prevent the destruction of his Army; this drastically reduced deaths by the disease, according to some accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenderson200947_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenderson200947-196">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> That summer, British General <a href="/wiki/John_Burgoyne" title="John Burgoyne">John Burgoyne</a> led a <a href="/wiki/Saratoga_campaign" title="Saratoga campaign">major invasion army</a> south from Quebec, planning to sever New England. But General Howe, in British occupied New York,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172_191-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172-191">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> made a strategic blunder and took his army <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_campaign" title="Philadelphia campaign">south to Philadelphia</a> instead of going up the Hudson River to join Burgoyne near Albany. Meanwhile, Washington rushed to Philadelphia to engage Howe, while he closely supervised the action in upstate New York where the Patriots were led by General <a href="/wiki/Philip_Schuyler" title="Philip Schuyler">Philip Schuyler</a> and his successor <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Gates" title="Horatio Gates">Horatio Gates</a>. The pitched battles at Philadelphia were too complex for Washington\'s less experienced men, and they were defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997340\xe2\x80\x93341_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997340\xe2\x80\x93341-197">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Howe outmaneuvered Washington at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brandywine" title="Battle of Brandywine">Battle of Brandywine</a> on September 11, 1777, and he marched unopposed into the American capital at Philadelphia. Washington\'s army <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Germantown" title="Battle of Germantown">unsuccessfully attacked</a> the British garrison at Germantown in early October, and his loss at Philadelphia prompted some members of Congress to consider removing him from command. His supporters rallied behind him, referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Conway_Cabal" title="Conway Cabal">Conway Cabal</a>, and the attempt failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeydt200550\xe2\x80\x9373_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeydt200550\xe2\x80\x9373-198">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, Washington\'s strategy improved the situation for Gates\' army during the Saratoga campaign to the north.  He was most concerned about the movements of General Howe and was aware that Burgoyne was also moving south toward Saratoga from Quebec. He took some risks in July and sent reinforcements north commanded by Major General Benedict Arnold, his most aggressive field commander, and Major General <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Lincoln" title="Benjamin Lincoln">Benjamin Lincoln</a>. Burgoyne made two attempts to take <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bemis_Heights" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Bemis Heights">Bemis Heights</a>, then found himself trapped and beyond the reach of help from Howe. He was forced to retreat to Saratoga and ultimately surrendered after the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga" title="Battles of Saratoga">Battles of Saratoga</a>, which led to Howe\'s resignation in May 1778. This was a major turning point militarily and diplomatically; the French responded to Burgoyne\'s defeat by entering the war, allying with America and expanding the Revolutionary War into a worldwide affair (from the perspective of the British).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010312\xe2\x80\x93314Higginbotham1971_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010312\xe2\x80\x93314Higginbotham1971-199">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington was concerned that Gate\'s victory was going to give impetus to his critics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010312\xe2\x80\x93313_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010312\xe2\x80\x93313-200">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Biographer Alden suggests that "it was inevitable that the defeats of Washington\'s forces and the concurrent victory of the forces in upper New York should be compared." The zealous admiration of Washington waned, with John Adams giving him little credit. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993163_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993163-201">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Valley_Forge.2C_Monmouth.2C_and_Southern_campaigns"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Valley_Forge,_Monmouth,_and_Southern_campaigns">Valley Forge, Monmouth, and Southern campaigns</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge" title="Valley Forge">Valley Forge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth" title="Battle of Monmouth">Battle of Monmouth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_theater_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War">Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg/220px-Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg/330px-Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg/440px-Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3362" data-file-height="2664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_and_Lafayette_at_Valley_Forge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Washington and Lafayette at Valley Forge</i><br />John Ward Dunsmore (1907)</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington\'s army of 11,000 went into winter quarters at <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge" title="Valley Forge">Valley Forge</a> north of Philadelphia in December 1777 and suffered thousands of deaths over the next six months. Most were from disease, compounded by lack of food and proper clothing, poor shelter, and the extreme cold. Historians\xe2\x80\x99 estimates range from 2,000 to more than 3,000 men lost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002186Alden1993165,_167Freedman200830_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002186Alden1993165,_167Freedman200830-202">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> The British, by contrast, were comfortably quartered in Philadelphia; they paid for their supplies in pounds sterling, while Washington had difficulty procuring supplies with depreciating American paper currency. The woodlands were soon exhausted of game, and Washington was faced with the task of maintaining morale and discouraging desertion by February.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993165_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993165-203">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington had repeatedly petitioned the Continental Congress for badly needed provisions but without success. Finally, five Congressmen came to Valley Forge on January 24, 1778 to check the conditions of the Continental Army. Washington expressed the urgency of the situation, exclaiming, "Something must be done. Important alterations must be made." He also recommended that Congress take control of the army supply system, pay for supplies, and hasten them to the troops. In response to his urgent appeal, Congress gave full support to funding the supply lines of the army, which also resulted in reorganizing the commissary department which gathered the supplies for the army. By late February, there were adequate supplies arriving at the camp.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedman200842_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman200842-154">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BattleofMonmouth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/BattleofMonmouth.jpg/220px-BattleofMonmouth.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/BattleofMonmouth.jpg/330px-BattleofMonmouth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/BattleofMonmouth.jpg/440px-BattleofMonmouth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BattleofMonmouth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Washington Rallying the Troops at Monmouth</i><br /><a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Leutze" title="Emanuel Leutze">Emanuel Leutze</a> (1851\xe2\x80\x931854)</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington recruited men into the regular army and assigned their training to Baron <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_von_Steuben" title="Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben">Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben</a>, whose incessant drilling soon transformed them into a disciplined fighting force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997342,_359Ferling2009172_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997342,_359Ferling2009172-204">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Washington\'s army had endured the winter that many feared would dissolve it, and a revitalized army emerged from Valley Forge the following spring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993168Randall1997342,_356_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993168Randall1997342,_356-205">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Von Steuben was promoted to Major General for his effort and became Washington\'s chief of staff for the rest of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010336_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010336-206">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 1778, the Continental Congress ratified the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alliance_(1778)" title="Treaty of Alliance (1778)">Treaty of Alliance</a> with King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XVI">Louis XVI</a> of France which allied the French military and navy with America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016188_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016188-207">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> The British evacuated Philadelphia for New York in June 1778, and Washington summoned a council of war with Generals <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lee_(general)" title="Charles Lee (general)">Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathanael_Greene" title="Nathanael Greene">Greene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Wayne" title="Anthony Wayne">Wayne</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Lafayette</a>. He chose a partial attack on the retreating British at the Battle of Monmouth; the British were commanded by Howe\'s successor General <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clinton_(British_Army_officer,_born_1730)" title="Henry Clinton (British Army officer, born 1730)">Henry Clinton</a>. Lee and Lafayette moved with 4,000 men and bungled their first attack on June 28, without Washington\'s knowledge. Washington relieved Lee and continued fighting to an effective draw in one of the war\'s largest battles. Nightfall came and the British continued their retreat to New York, where Washington moved his army outside the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993176\xe2\x80\x9377Ferling2002195\xe2\x80\x9397_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993176\xe2\x80\x9377Ferling2002195\xe2\x80\x9397-208">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Monmouth was the last major battle that Washington fought in the north; he deemed it more important to protect his army than to keep the British from occupying towns which rarely had anything to offer the British army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010344_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010344-209">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late 1778, General Clinton sent 3,000 troops by ship from New York to Georgia and launched a Southern invasion. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230-210">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> He seized Savannah, reinforced by 2,000 British and Loyalist troops, and repelled an attack by Patriots and French naval forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230-210">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> The success bolstered the British War effort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230_210-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230-210">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Clinton assembled 12,500 troops and attacked Charlestown in January 1780, defeating General Lincoln who only had 5,100 Continental troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234-211">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> The British went on to occupy the South Carolina Piedmont in June, with almost no Patriot resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234-211">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Clinton returned to New York and left 8,000 troops commanded by Lord Cornwallis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234\xe2\x80\x93235_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234\xe2\x80\x93235-212">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Congress replaced Lincoln with Gates, despite Washington\'s recommendation of Greene. Gates failed in South Carolina and was replaced by Greene, and the British had the South in their grasp. Despite this bleak situation, Washington was encouraged when he learned in mid-1780 that Lafayette had returned from France with more ships, men, and supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993187\xe2\x80\x93188_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993187\xe2\x80\x93188-213">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sullivan_expedition_and_Hudson_River">Sullivan expedition and Hudson River</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sullivan_Expedition" title="Sullivan Expedition">Sullivan Expedition</a></div>\n<p>In the summer of 1779, Washington and Congress decided to strike the <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> warriors of the "Six Nations" in a campaign to force Britain\'s Indian allies out of New York, which they had used as a base to attack American settlements around New England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002303_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2002303-214">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The Indian warriors joined with Tory rangers led by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Butler_(Loyalist)" title="Walter Butler (Loyalist)">Walter Butler</a> and slew more than 200 frontiersmen in June, using barbarities normally shunned, and they laid waste to the <a href="/wiki/Wyoming_Valley" title="Wyoming Valley">Wyoming Valley</a> in Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993184_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993184-215">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Washington ordered General John Sullivan to lead a <a href="/wiki/Sullivan_Expedition" title="Sullivan Expedition">military operation</a> in August, prompted by massacres and many attacks on American civilians, and to affect "the total destruction and devastation" of all Iroquois villages and to take their women and children as hostages. Those who managed to escape fled to British protection in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010360_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010360-216">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Sullivan later reported that the expedition had been accomplished, referring to the Iroquois as "inhuman barbarians".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMann2008106_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMann2008106-217">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington went into quarters at Morristown, New Jersey during the harsh winter of 1779\xe2\x80\x9380, which subjected the troops to some of the worst suffering during the war with temperatures well below freezing. New York Harbor was frozen over, and snow and ice covered the ground for weeks, with the troops again lacking provisions for a time as at Valley Forge.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Point_espionage">West Point espionage</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/West_Point" class="mw-redirect" title="West Point">West Point&#160;</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_career_of_Benedict_Arnold,_1777%E2%80%9379" title="Military career of Benedict Arnold, 1777\xe2\x80\x9379">&#160;Military career of Benedict Arnold, 1777\xe2\x80\x9379</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Culper_Ring_code.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Culper_Ring_code.jpg/170px-Culper_Ring_code.jpg" width="170" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Culper_Ring_code.jpg/255px-Culper_Ring_code.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Culper_Ring_code.jpg/340px-Culper_Ring_code.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1720" data-file-height="2752" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Culper_Ring_code.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <a href="/wiki/Culper_Ring" title="Culper Ring">Culper Ring</a>\'s codebook, listing the men whom Washington gathered to be agents</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington had worked hard to develop a successful espionage system to detect British locations and plans, and he has been called "Americas First Spymaster".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENagy2016274_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENagy2016274-218">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> In 1778, he ordered Major <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Tallmadge" title="Benjamin Tallmadge">Benjamin Tallmadge</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Culper_Ring" title="Culper Ring">Culper Ring</a> to collect information about the British in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose200675,_224,_258\xe2\x80\x9361_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose200675,_224,_258\xe2\x80\x9361-219">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> He was usually mindful of treachery, but he ignored incidents of disloyalty by <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a>, his admired and trusted officer who had distinguished himself in many battles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010378\xe2\x80\x93387Ward1994_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010378\xe2\x80\x93387Ward1994-220">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>  Several factors which probably combined to motivated Arnold. He received a \xc2\xa36,000 British payment; he was angry at losing promotions to junior officers and at the repeated slights from Congress;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer2010203Flexner1991119\xe2\x80\x93221Rose2006196Taylor2016206_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer2010203Flexner1991119\xe2\x80\x93221Rose2006196Taylor2016206-221">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> he was profiteering from the war and faced a court martial for it; and he was deeply in debt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams1928365Palmer2010306,_315,_319,_320_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1928365Palmer2010306,_315,_319,_320-222">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the summer of 1780, Arnold began his plot of treason, supplying Andr\xc3\xa9 with sensitive information intended to compromise Washington and capture West Point, a key American defensive position on the Hudson River.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams1928365\xe2\x80\x93366Philbrick2016250\xe2\x80\x93251Ward1994_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1928365\xe2\x80\x93366Philbrick2016250\xe2\x80\x93251Ward1994-223">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Arnold repeatedly asked for command of West Point, and Washington finally relented and gave it to him in August.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010378,_380\xe2\x80\x93381Lengel2005322Adams1928366Philbrick2016280\xe2\x80\x93282_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010378,_380\xe2\x80\x93381Lengel2005322Adams1928366Philbrick2016280\xe2\x80\x93282-224">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Arnold met  British spymaster <a href="/wiki/John_Andr%C3%A9" title="John Andr\xc3\xa9">John Andr\xc3\xa9</a> on September 21 and gave him the plans to take over the garrison; but Andr\xc3\xa9 was captured by militia forces who discovered Arnold\'s plans. With only minutes to make good his escape, Arnold took flight on horseback and escaped to New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Doren1941194\xe2\x80\x93195Adams1928366Palmer2010410_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Doren1941194\xe2\x80\x93195Adams1928366Palmer2010410-225">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An outraged Washington immediately recalled all the commanders under Arnold at key positions around the fort as a precaution against any complicity on their part; however, he did not suspect Arnold\'s wife at this time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer2010370Middlekauff2015232_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer2010370Middlekauff2015232-226">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Washington assumed personal command of West Point and worked earnestly reorganizing the order of command and strengthening defensive positions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer2010371_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer2010371-227">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Andr\xc3\xa9 was tried by a military court for spying and was sentenced to death. Washington offered to return him to the British in exchange for Arnold, but Clinton refused. Andr\xc3\xa9 then asked Washington to be executed by firing squad, and Washington was inclined to grant his wish; but he changed his mind under pressure to make an example of the enemy spymaster. Andr\xc3\xa9 was hanged on October 2, 1780.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlexner1991386Rose2006212_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1991386Rose2006212-228">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Yorktown_victory.2C_peace_treaty.2C_and_resignation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Yorktown_victory,_peace_treaty,_and_resignation">Yorktown victory, peace treaty, and resignation</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Southern_theater_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War">Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">Siege of Yorktown</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG/220px-Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG/330px-Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG/440px-Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Couder_Yorktown_Versailles.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">Siege of Yorktown</a>, Generals Washington and <a href="/wiki/Comte_de_Rochambeau" class="mw-redirect" title="Comte de Rochambeau">Rochambeau</a> give last orders before the attack</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington\'s army went into winter quarters at <a href="/wiki/New_Windsor,_New_York" title="New Windsor, New York">New Windsor, New York</a> in 1780 and suffered again from extreme cold and lack of supplies, prompting Washington to prevail upon Congress and state officials to come to their aid with provisions. He sympathized with their suffering, saying that he hoped that the army would not "continue to struggle under the same difficulties they have hitherto endured".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993193_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993193-229">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 1780, 5,000 veteran French troops led by the <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Donatien_de_Vimeur,_comte_de_Rochambeau" title="Jean-Baptiste Donatien de Vimeur, comte de Rochambeau"><i>comte</i> de Rochambeau</a> arrived at <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELancasterPlumb1985311_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELancasterPlumb1985311-230">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> French naval forces then landed, led by Admiral <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Joseph_Paul_de_Grasse" title="Fran\xc3\xa7ois Joseph Paul de Grasse">Fran\xc3\xa7ois Joseph Paul de Grasse</a>, and Washington encouraged Rochambeau to move his fleet south, to launch a joint land-naval attack on Arnold\'s troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993197\xe2\x80\x93199,206_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993197\xe2\x80\x93199,206-231">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI,_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_(1754-1793),_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3592" data-file-height="5062" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI,_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_(1754-1793),_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XVI">Louis XVI</a> allied with Washington and Patriot American colonists.</div></div></div>\n<p>Arnold was now a Brigadier General in the British Army, and General Clinton sent him to Virginia with about 1,700 troops to capture <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_Virginia" title="Portsmouth, Virginia">Portsmouth</a> and spread terror throughout the state; Washington responded by sending Lafayette south with a small army to counter Arnold\'s efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010403_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010403-232">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> At first, Washington hoped to bring the allied fight to New York, drawing off British forces from Virginia and ending the war there, but Rochambeau advised de Grasse that Cornwallis in Virginia was the better target. Admiral de Grasse followed this advice and arrived with his fleet off the Virginia coast. Washington immediately saw the advantage created, made a feinting move with his force towards Clinton in New York, and then headed south to Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993198\xe2\x80\x9399Chernow2010403\xe2\x80\x93404_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993198\xe2\x80\x9399Chernow2010403\xe2\x80\x93404-233">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s Continental Army delivered the last blow in 1781, bolstered by $20,000 in French gold and troops. The French won a naval victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Chesapeake" title="Battle of the Chesapeake">Battle of the Chesapeake</a>, allowing Patriot forces to trap the British army in Virginia and prevent reinforcement by Clinton from the North,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993198,_201Chernow2010372\xe2\x80\x93373,_418_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993198,_201Chernow2010372\xe2\x80\x93373,_418-234">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">surrender at Yorktown</a> on October 19, 1781 marked the end of major fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMann200838LancasterPlumb1985254Chernow2010419_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMann200838LancasterPlumb1985254Chernow2010419-235">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Washington took great satisfaction in the surrender but displayed no outward sign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiddlekauff2015276_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiddlekauff2015276-236">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Cornwallis failed to appear at the official surrender ceremony, claiming illness; he sent General <a href="/wiki/Charles_O%27Hara" title="Charles O&#39;Hara">Charles O\'Hara</a> as his proxy. Washington then had General Benjamin Lincoln accept the surrender in his place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993201\xe2\x80\x9302_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993201\xe2\x80\x9302-237">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Decisive combat had ended, and British troops began to demobilize months after Yorktown, while peace negotiations started.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016313_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016313-238">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> The British evacuated 2,000 troops from Savannah in July 1782 and 4,000 from Charles Town in December.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016313-314_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016313-314-239">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> They removed 18,000 troops from New York throughout the Spring, Summer, and Fall of 1783,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016315_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016315-240">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> and the French army and navy departed. The American treasury was empty, and unpaid soldiers were restive to the point of mutiny and forced an adjournment of the Congress. Washington dispelled unrest among officers by suppressing the <a href="/wiki/Newburgh_Conspiracy" title="Newburgh Conspiracy">Newburgh Conspiracy</a> in March 1783, and Congress promised the officers a five-year bonus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn1970187\xe2\x80\x93220_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1970187\xe2\x80\x93220-241">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Washington later submitted a formal detailed account of about $450,000 in military expenses that he advanced to the army in his tenure. It was allegedly vague about large sums, and included wife Martha\'s expenses incurred in visits to his headquarters as well as his agreed compensation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993209_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993209-242">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg/220px-General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg/330px-General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg/440px-General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1962" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:General_George_Washington_Resigning_his_Commission.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/General_George_Washington_Resigning_His_Commission" title="General George Washington Resigning His Commission">General George Washington Resigning His Commission</a></i><br /><a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>, 1824</div></div></div>\n<p>With the peace treaty initially ratified in April 1783, a Congressional committee under Hamilton was arranging a peacetime army. On May 2, 1783, Washington submitted the Army\xe2\x80\x99s position to the Committee in his <i>Sentiments on a Peace Establishment</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington1783_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington1783-243">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> The Committee\xe2\x80\x99s proposals were defeated in Congress by votes taken in May 1783, October 1783, and April 1784.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWrightMacGregor198727_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWrightMacGregor198727-244">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> was signed on September 3, 1783, and Great Britain officially recognized the independence of the United States. Washington disbanded his army and gave an eloquent farewell address to his soldiers on November 2.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington1799343_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington1799343-245">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> On November 25, the <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_Day_(New_York)" title="Evacuation Day (New York)">British evacuated New York City</a>, and Washington and the governor took possession. Only a few trusted members of Congress, including Jefferson, knew of Washington\'s decision to <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_resignation_as_commander-in-chief" title="George Washington&#39;s resignation as commander-in-chief">resign his commission</a> as Commander-in-chief.\n</p><p>Washington bade farewell to his officers at <a href="/wiki/Fraunces_Tavern" title="Fraunces Tavern">Fraunces Tavern</a> on December 4, 1783<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010417_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010417-246">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> and resigned his commission on December 23, after leading the Continental Army for 8\xc2\xbd years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997402Chernow2010416_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997402Chernow2010416-247">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> He gave a brief statement to the Continental Congress in the Senate Chamber of the Maryland State House: "I consider it an indispensable duty to close this last solemn act of my official life, by commending the interests of our dearest country to the protection of Almighty God, and those who have the superintendence of them, to his holy keeping."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993210_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993210-248">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Gordon_S._Wood" title="Gordon S. Wood">Gordon S. Wood</a> concludes that "the greatest act of his life, the one that gave him his greatest fame, was his resignation as commander in chief of the American forces."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1992205_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1992205-249">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>  Washington likened himself to the heroic Roman consul <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Quintus_Cincinnatus" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucius Quintus Cincinnatus">Lucius Quintus Cincinnatus</a>, who relinquished his military power after securing victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444,_461Ferling2009xxParsons189896Brumwell2012412_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444,_461Ferling2009xxParsons189896Brumwell2012412-250">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Henry Knox formed the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Cincinnati" title="Society of the Cincinnati">Society of the Cincinnati</a> in 1783 with this connection in mind, and Washington served as its first president until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington advised Congress at <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Hill,_New_Jersey" title="Rocky Hill, New Jersey">Rocky Hill, New Jersey</a> in August 1783 to keep a standing army, create a "national militia" formed of separate state units, and establish a navy and a national military academy. He circulated his "Farewell" orders that discharged his troops, whom he called "one patriotic band of brothers." Before his return to Mount Vernon, he oversaw the evacuation of British forces in New York, and was greeted by parades and celebrations where he announced that Knox was promoted the new commander of the Continental Army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010446,_448\xe2\x80\x93449,_451Puls2008184\xe2\x80\x93186_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010446,_448\xe2\x80\x93449,_451Puls2008184\xe2\x80\x93186-251">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington made an exploratory trip to the western frontier in 1784 after his retirement and return to Mount Vernon, and he inspected his land holdings that had been earned decades earlier for his service in the French and Indian War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell200552,_66_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell200552,_66-252">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> He also facilitated the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_Company" title="Potomac Company">Potomac Company</a>, a public\xe2\x80\x93private partnership which financed a project to improve the navigability of the Potomac River and to construct a canal linking the Potomac to the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. He was elected president of the company, for which he proselytized extensively. The project served as a model for large-scale canal building, but technical and financial challenges rendered it unprofitable, and the Potomac-Ohio canal was not completed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009251\xe2\x80\x93255_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009251\xe2\x80\x93255-253">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_Convention">Constitutional Convention</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention (United States)</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1788%E2%80%9389" title="United States presidential election, 1788\xe2\x80\x9389">United States presidential election, 1788\xe2\x80\x9389</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg/280px-Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg" width="280" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg/420px-Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg/560px-Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1933" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Scene_at_the_Signing_of_the_Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Scene at the Signing of the Constitution of the United States">Scene at the Signing of the Constitution of the United States</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Howard_Chandler_Christy" title="Howard Chandler Christy">Howard Chandler Christy</a>, 1940. Washington, presiding officer, stands at right.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the War, Washington did not wish to involve himself in the political matters of the nation. <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, however, valued his influence and attempted to persuade him to attend the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention</a>. <a href="/wiki/Shay%27s_rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Shay&#39;s rebellion">Shay\'s rebellion</a> broke out in Massachusetts and Washington was finally convinced that he could no longer ignore political matters and the looming unrest which threatened the stability of the Union. He appeared at the convention as a delegate from Virginia, and he was unanimously elected its president in 1787.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010516\xe2\x80\x93517Unger201333_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010516\xe2\x80\x93517Unger201333-254">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> He was critical of the <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a> for the weak central government which it established, referring to them as no more than "a rope of sand" to unite the new nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993221_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993221-255">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> His view for the need of a strong federal government developed from his early years of frustration with British officials and his experience at Valley Forge when the Continental Congress failed to supply the military. The general populace, however, did not share his inclination for a strong federal government binding the states together, fearing that it would become as overbearing as the British Parliament from which they had just freed themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis200791\xe2\x80\x9392_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200791\xe2\x80\x9392-256">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington was reserved during the debates and voting, lending his prestige to the goodwill and work of the other delegates. After a couple of months, he wrote to Alexander Hamilton expressing his anxiety that he was the only one holding the union of delegates together. "I almost despair of seeing a favorable issue to the proceedings of our convention and do therefore repent having had any agency in the business."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993226\xe2\x80\x9327Lodge188934\xe2\x80\x9335_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993226\xe2\x80\x9327Lodge188934\xe2\x80\x9335-257">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Following the Convention, however, his support convinced many to vote for ratification of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a>. He unsuccessfully lobbied anti-federalist <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a>, saying that "the adoption of it under the present circumstances of the Union is in my opinion desirable", and he declared that the only alternative would be anarchy. Nevertheless, he did not consider it proper to cast his vote in favor of adoption on behalf of Virginia as the state\'s representative, since he was expected to be nominated President if it was ratified.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993229_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993229-258">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Washington and Madison then retired to Mount Vernon for four days and evaluated the transition of the new constitutional government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010545\xe2\x80\x93546_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010545\xe2\x80\x93546-259">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The delegates to the convention designed the presidency with Washington in mind, allowing him to define the office once elected. He thought that the achievements were monumental when they were finally completed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlden1993226\xe2\x80\x9327_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1993226\xe2\x80\x9327-260">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup> The delegates counted slaves to represent three-fifths of the population, which increased Southern representation in the House and <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010136\xe2\x80\x93137_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010136\xe2\x80\x93137-264">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The state electors under the Constitution voted for the President on February 4, 1789. Washington suspected that most Republican electors had not voted for him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010559\xe2\x80\x93560Ferling2009361_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010559\xe2\x80\x93560Ferling2009361-265">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> The March 4 date mandated by the Constitution passed by without a Congressional quorum to count the votes, and Congress waited anxiously for other members to arrive to determine who won the election. A quorum was finally reached on April 5, and Congress counted the votes on April 6.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010551_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010551-266">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Congressional Secretary <a href="/wiki/Charles_Thomson" title="Charles Thomson">Charles Thomson</a> was sent to Mount Vernon to tell Washington that he had been elected President of the United States. Washington won the majority of every state\'s electoral votes, while Adams received the next highest vote total and was elected Vice President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274-267">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Washington had "anxious and painful sensations" over leaving the "domestic felicity" of  Mount Vernon, but he departed Mount Vernon for New York City on April 23 to be inaugurated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274\xe2\x80\x93275Chernow2010559\xe2\x80\x93561_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274\xe2\x80\x93275Chernow2010559\xe2\x80\x93561-268">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Presidency_.281789.E2.80.931797.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_(1789\xe2\x80\x931797)">Presidency (1789\xe2\x80\x931797)</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington" title="Presidency of George Washington">Presidency of George Washington</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Washington_1795.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/George_Washington_1795.jpg/200px-George_Washington_1795.jpg" width="200" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/George_Washington_1795.jpg/300px-George_Washington_1795.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/George_Washington_1795.jpg/400px-George_Washington_1795.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3105" data-file-height="3710" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Washington_1795.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><i>President George Washington</i></center>, Gilbert Stuart 1795</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington was <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_George_Washington" title="First inauguration of George Washington">inaugurated</a> on April 30, 1789 at age 57, taking the first presidential <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States" title="Oath of office of the President of the United States">oath of office</a> at <a href="/wiki/Federal_Hall" title="Federal Hall">Federal Hall</a> in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20024Fitzpatrick1936V19:_522_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024Fitzpatrick1936V19:_522-269">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup> He arrived in a coach led by militia and a marching band, followed by statesmen and foreign dignitaries in the first inaugural parade; an estimated 10,000 people attended.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010566\xe2\x80\x93567Randall1997448_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010566\xe2\x80\x93567Randall1997448-272">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> He stood with his hand on a Bible that was provided by the nearby Masonic lodge while the oath was administered by Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Livingston_(chancellor)" title="Robert R. Livingston (chancellor)">Robert R. Livingston</a>, after which he was given a 13-gun salute.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20024Chernow2010568_273-0" class="reference"><a
