href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024Chernow2010568-273">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> He then returned to the Senate Chambers where he read a 1,200\xe2\x80\x93word speech, asking that an "Almighty Being" bless the "important revolution".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997448Alden1993236_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997448Alden1993236-274">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> He declined a salary in his speech, but Congress later set an annual salary of $25,000 and he accepted the amount to defray costs of the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010552Fitzpatrick1936V19:_522_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010552Fitzpatrick1936V19:_522-275">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Washington was aware that he would set a precedent with everything that he said and did, and he attended carefully to the pomp and ceremony of office, making sure that the titles and trappings were suitably republican and did not emulate European royal courts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnger201379_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger201379-276">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;m&#93;</a></sup> To that end, he preferred the title "Mr. President" over more majestic names proposed by the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBassett1906155_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBassett1906155-278">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> His precedents also included messages to Congress and the cabinet form of the executive branch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337-279">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>New political parties emerged unexpectedly, which troubled Washington, and his two terms of office became known as the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Era" title="Federalist Era">Federalist Era</a>, when the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> dominated American politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanning19742_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19742-280">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> He had planned to resign after his first term, but the unstable nation with its existing political strife convinced him that he should remain in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010674\xe2\x80\x93675_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010674\xe2\x80\x93675-281">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> He was an able administrator and judge of talent and character and established many precedents; he talked regularly with department heads and listened to their advice before making a final decision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2004197\xe2\x80\x9398Unger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004197\xe2\x80\x9398Unger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337-282">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> He established a tolerance of opposing views, despite fears that a democratic system would lead to political violence, and he conducted a smooth transition of power to his successor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKazin2009589Unger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKazin2009589Unger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337-283">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During his first term in office, Washington had to contend with major problems. The old Confederation had lacked the powers to handle its workload. It had weak leadership, no executive, a small bureaucracy of clerks, a large debt, worthless paper money, and no power to establish taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20025_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-284">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> The United States was not completely unified, and Washington had the task of assembling an executive department; he relied on <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Lear" title="Tobias Lear">Tobias Lear</a> for advice selecting its officers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010575_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010575-285">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Great Britain also refused to relinquish its forts in the American West,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20025_284-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-284">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> the United States Army was minuscule, and the United States Navy had not materialized.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinet_and_executive_departments">Cabinet and executive departments</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_and_His_Cabinet_(4360094798).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Washington_and_His_Cabinet_%284360094798%29.jpg/220px-Washington_and_His_Cabinet_%284360094798%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Washington_and_His_Cabinet_%284360094798%29.jpg/330px-Washington_and_His_Cabinet_%284360094798%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Washington_and_His_Cabinet_%284360094798%29.jpg/440px-Washington_and_His_Cabinet_%284360094798%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1520" data-file-height="1140" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_and_His_Cabinet_(4360094798).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Washington and his cabinet: (left to right) Washington, Knox, Hamilton (standing), Jefferson, and Randolph</div></div></div>\n<p>Congress created executive departments during Washington\'s first months in office in 1789, including the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a> on July 27, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">Department of War</a> in early August, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Department of Treasury">Treasury Department</a> on September 2. The President also received two additional officers without departments: the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postmaster_General" title="United States Postmaster General">Postmaster General</a>. Washington appointed Richmond lawyer <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Randolph" title="Edmund Randolph">Edmund Randolph</a> as Attorney General and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Osgood" title="Samuel Osgood">Samuel Osgood</a> as Postmaster General. He also appointed fellow Virginian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> to be Secretary of State and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Knox" title="Henry Knox">Henry Knox</a> as Secretary of War. Finally, he appointed <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> to head the Treasury Department.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20024_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024-286">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Washington\'s cabinet eventually developed into a consultation and advisory body, although this was not mandated by the Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20024\xe2\x80\x935_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024\xe2\x80\x935-287">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s cabinet members were known for their dissension, forming rival parties with sharply opposing views, most fiercely illustrated between Hamilton and Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20025Banning19745_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025Banning19745-288">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Washington restricted cabinet discussions to topics of his choosing, without participating in debate. He occasionally requested cabinet opinions in writing, and he expected his department heads to carry out his decisions without complaint. Hamilton played an active role advising Congress, including written reports and using influence over congressional committee leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20025_284-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-284">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_issues">Domestic issues</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg/220px-Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg/330px-Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg/440px-Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="816" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The President\'s House in Philadelphia was Washington\'s residence from 1790 to 1797</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington was not aligned with a political party and opposed their formation, fearing that conflict that would undermine republicanism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995290_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995290-289">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> His closest advisors formed two factions, however, setting the framework for the future <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a>. Secretary of the Treasury Alexander Hamilton had plans to establish the national credit and to build a financially powerful nation, and he formed the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>. Secretary of State Thomas Jefferson was the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Jeffersonian Republicans</a>, and he strenuously opposed Hamilton\'s agenda. Washington favored Hamilton\'s agenda, which went into effect and became law, but Hamilton\'s fiscal recommendations created bitter controversy during Washington\'s presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20027_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20027-290">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington signed a proclamation on October 3, 1789 making November 26, 1789 to be the first Thanksgiving in order to encourage national unity. He said, "It is the duty of all nations to acknowledge the providence of Almighty God, to obey His will, to be grateful for His benefits, and humbly to implore His protection and favor." On his appointed Thanksgiving Day, he fasted while visiting debtors in prison, providing them with food and beer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010585,_609Henriques200665Novak2007144\xe2\x80\x93146_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010585,_609Henriques200665Novak2007144\xe2\x80\x93146-291">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Bank">National Bank</span></h4>\n<p>The establishment of public credit was primary task of the new federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanning19745-7_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19745-7-292">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> On January 14, 1790, Hamilton submitted a report to Congress on the matter. Madison, Hamilton, and Jefferson then reached the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1790" title="Compromise of 1790">Compromise of 1790</a> in which Jefferson agreed to Hamilton\xe2\x80\x99s debt proposals in exchange for moving the nation\xe2\x80\x99s capitol to the south near <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Georgetown (Washington, D.C.)">Georgetown</a> on the Potomac River.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20027_290-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20027-290">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> This settled a deadlock in Congress, and the terms were legislated in the <a href="/wiki/Funding_Act_of_1790" title="Funding Act of 1790">Funding Act</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Residence_Act" title="Residence Act">Residence Act</a>, both of which Washington signed into law on August 4. Congress authorized the assumption and payment of the nation\xe2\x80\x99s debts, providing funding through customs duties and excise taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20027\xe2\x80\x938_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20027\xe2\x80\x938-293">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hamilton created more controversy among Washington\'s Cabinet members when he advocated the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">First Bank of the United States</a>. Madison and Jefferson objected, but the bank easily passed Congress. Washington sought advice from his cabinet; Jefferson and Randolph strongly opposed the new bank constitutionally, while Hamilton believed that it was within the authority of the government\'s enumerated powers. Washington sided with Hamilton and signed the legislation on February 25; however, the rift widened between Hamilton and Jefferson and became openly hostile.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20028_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20028-294">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under Washington, the young nation experienced its <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1792" title="Panic of 1792">first financial crisis</a> in March 1792 when federalists under Hamilton used large loans to gain control of U.S. debt securities, causing a run on the new national bank,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel196827_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel196827-295">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> but the markets returned to normal by mid-April.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanning19749Sobel196830_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19749Sobel196830-296">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> Jefferson believed that Hamilton was part of the scheme, in spite of Hamilton\'s efforts to ameliorate, and Washington found himself caught in the middle of a feud.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010673\xe2\x80\x93674_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010673\xe2\x80\x93674-297">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Jefferson.E2.80.93Hamilton_feud"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jefferson\xe2\x80\x93Hamilton_feud">Jefferson\xe2\x80\x93Hamilton feud</span></h4>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:beige">Jefferson and Hamilton, bitter rivals</div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:140px;max-width:140px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/138px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" width="138" height="194" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/207px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/276px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="506" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><center>Thomas Jefferson</center></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806,_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg/162px-Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg" width="162" height="192" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg/243px-Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg/324px-Alexander_Hamilton_portrait_by_John_Trumbull_1806%2C_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1650" data-file-height="1959" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><center>Alexander Hamilton</center></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson and Hamilton staked out diametrically opposed political principles. Hamilton believed in a strong national government that needed a national bank and foreign loans to function, while Jefferson believed that the government should be primarily under the direction of the states and the farm element, and he deeply resented the idea of banks and foreign loans. This difference was the cause of continued disputes and infighting between the two, much to Washington\'s dismay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010515,_627\xe2\x80\x93630,_648\xe2\x80\x93650Randall1997452,_463,_468\xe2\x80\x93471_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010515,_627\xe2\x80\x93630,_648\xe2\x80\x93650Randall1997452,_463,_468\xe2\x80\x93471-298">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> In 1791, Jefferson and Congressman <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> encouraged revolutionary poet <a href="/wiki/Philip_Freneau" title="Philip Freneau">Philip Freneau</a> to form the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Gazette" title="National Gazette">National Gazette</a></i>, a newspaper to counter the pro-Hamilton press.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanning19746Ferling2013221\xe2\x80\x93222_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19746Ferling2013221\xe2\x80\x93222-299">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> A few weeks later, Hamilton demanded that Jefferson resign if he could not support Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanning19748_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19748-300">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> Rather than respond publicly, Jefferson told Washington that Hamilton\'s fiscal system would lead to the overthrow of the republic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanning19748Cooke20029_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19748Cooke20029-301">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington pleaded with his two secretaries by letters to stop the open warfare for the sake of the nation, but they both politely ignored the president\'s advice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20029Fitzpatrick1936V19:_523_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20029Fitzpatrick1936V19:_523-302">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> Washington reluctantly reversed his decision to retire after his first term when it became clear that this would be essential in minimizing party strife, but the feud continued after his re-election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanning19748Cooke20029_301-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanning19748Cooke20029-301">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Jefferson\'s political actions, his support of Freneau\'s <i>National Gazette</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995240,_285,_290,_361_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995240,_285,_290,_361-303">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> and his attempt to undermine Hamilton nearly led Washington to dismiss him from the cabinet; Jefferson ultimately resigned his position in December 1793 and was thereafter forsaken by Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke20029Chernow2005427_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20029Chernow2005427-304">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> The feud between Hamilton and Jefferson led to the well-defined Federalist and Republican parties, and party affiliation was necessary for election to Congress by 1794.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2013222,_283\xe2\x80\x93284,_301\xe2\x80\x93302_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2013222,_283\xe2\x80\x93284,_301\xe2\x80\x93302-305">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington remained aloof from congressional attacks on Hamilton, but he did not write a public statement to protect him. The <a href="/wiki/Hamilton%E2%80%93Reynolds_sex_scandal" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamilton\xe2\x80\x93Reynolds sex scandal">Hamilton\xe2\x80\x93Reynolds sex scandal</a> embarrassed Hamilton, but Washington held him in "very high esteem" and viewed him as the dominant force in establishing federal law and government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2013301\xe2\x80\x93302_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2013301\xe2\x80\x93302-306">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Whiskey_Rebellion">Whiskey Rebellion</span></h4>\n<p>In March 1791, Congress imposed an excise tax on distilled spirits to help pay the national debt; grain farmers strongly protested in frontier districts, especially the westernmost counties of Pennsylvania, saying that they were unrepresented and were unfairly shouldering too much of the debt. Threats and violence against tax collectors escalated into full-scale defiance of federal authority in 1794 known as the <a href="/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion" title="Whiskey Rebellion">Whiskey Rebellion</a>. The federal army was too small to meet the task, so Washington invoked the <a href="/wiki/Militia_Act_of_1792" class="mw-redirect" title="Militia Act of 1792">Militia Act of 1792</a> to summon militias from Pennsylvania, Virginia, Maryland, and New Jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoakley199643\xe2\x80\x9349_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoakley199643\xe2\x80\x9349-307">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> The governors sent the troops, with Washington taking initial command. He subsequently named <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lee_III" title="Henry Lee III">Henry "Lighthorse Harry" Lee</a> as field commander to lead the troops into the rebellious districts. The rebels dispersed and there was no fighting. Washington\'s forceful action demonstrated that the new government could protect itself and its tax collectors. This represented the premier instance of the federal government using military force to exert authority over the states and citizens<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn1972567\xe2\x80\x9384_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1972567\xe2\x80\x9384-308">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> and was the only time that a sitting president personally commanded troops in the field. Washington justified his action against "certain self-created societies" whom he regarded as "subversive organizations" which threatened the national union. He was not disputing their right to protest, but insisted that their dissent should not take the form of flagrant violation of federal law. Congress overwhelmingly agreed and extended their congratulations to him, with only Madison and Jefferson expressing their indifference.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2004225\xe2\x80\x93226_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004225\xe2\x80\x93226-309">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_affairs">Indian affairs</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fallen_timbers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Fallen_timbers.jpg/220px-Fallen_timbers.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Fallen_timbers.jpg/330px-Fallen_timbers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Fallen_timbers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="439" data-file-height="286" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fallen_timbers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Battle of Fallen Timbers</i> by R. F. Zogbaum, 1896. The <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">Ohio Country</a> was ceded to America in its aftermath.</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington\'s most pressing problem at the start of his presidency was the British occupation of forts in the Northwest frontier<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_523_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_523-310">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> and their <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War" title="Northwest Indian War">concerted efforts</a> to turn Indians against American settlers. The British were also protecting their interests in the Canadian fur trade, while Indian tribes warred with one other over hunting ground. Washington told Northwest Territory Governor <a href="/wiki/Arthur_St._Clair" title="Arthur St. Clair">Arthur St. Clair</a> as early as 1789 that he preferred a peace treaty with the hostile Indians of the Ohio Country. Tribes of Indians formed a confederacy led by <a href="/wiki/Little_Turtle" title="Little Turtle">Little Turtle</a> and other chiefs and were conducting numerous attacks and depredations against settlers, and they roamed largely uncontested throughout the territory. In 1790, Washington sent Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Harmar" title="Josiah Harmar">Josiah Harmar</a> to control the situation, but he was defeated twice and forced to withdraw.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010667\xe2\x80\x93678Gaff2004xvii_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010667\xe2\x80\x93678Gaff2004xvii-311">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Indian Confederacy used guerrilla tactics and was an effective force against the sparsely manned American army, composed mostly of undisciplined militia, some with their families along. Washington sent St. Clair from Fort Washington on an expedition to restore peace in the territory in 1791, with the encouragement of General Knox who disliked militias. On November 4, St. Clair\'s forces were ambushed and overwhelmingly <a href="/wiki/St._Clair%27s_Defeat" title="St. Clair&#39;s Defeat">defeated</a> with few survivors, despite Washington\'s warning of surprise attacks. Washington was outraged over the Indians\' brutality and the fact that they executed captured survivors, including women and children, and Knox and others prompted him to form a <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_the_United_States" title="Legion of the United States">new army</a> that didn\'t rely on state militias.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaff20043\xe2\x80\x936Ferling2009340_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaff20043\xe2\x80\x936Ferling2009340-312">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Congress vindicated St. Clair\'s management of the battle restore the Army\'s reputation, but Washington forced his resignation and replaced him with Revolutionary War hero General <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Wayne" title="Anthony Wayne">Anthony Wayne</a>, who instructed his troops on Indian warfare tactics. He was determined to instill discipline in the army which was lacking under St. Clair, and he sometimes shaved, branded, or whipped soldiers to sharpen their performance;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke200210Chernow2010668_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210Chernow2010668-313">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> three men were even executed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009340_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009340-314">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> On August 24, 1794, Wayne defeated the Northwest Indians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fallen_Timbers" title="Battle of Fallen Timbers">Battle of Fallen Timbers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke200214_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200214-315">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Indians had been given numerous peace offers in an effort to co-exist with settlers. Indians were also given livestock and farm tools, but these were rejected also, while the British continued their efforts to incite Indian raids on American settlers. Finally Wayne was sent into the troubled territory with orders to drive the hostile Indians out by burning their villages and crops in the <a href="/wiki/Maumee,_Ohio" title="Maumee, Ohio">Maumee Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2016406Chernow2010668_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016406Chernow2010668-316">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> In August 1795, under the  <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Greenville" title="Treaty of Greenville">Treaty of Greenville</a>, two-thirds of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">Ohio Country</a> was opened up for American cession and settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke200214Taylor2016406_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200214Taylor2016406-317">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>   Washington equated killing Indians with killing whites, and he desired a policy to protect their property and integrate Indians into American culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke200210_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210-318">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Jay_(Gilbert_Stuart_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/John_Jay_%28Gilbert_Stuart_portrait%29.jpg/170px-John_Jay_%28Gilbert_Stuart_portrait%29.jpg" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/John_Jay_%28Gilbert_Stuart_portrait%29.jpg/255px-John_Jay_%28Gilbert_Stuart_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/John_Jay_%28Gilbert_Stuart_portrait%29.jpg/340px-John_Jay_%28Gilbert_Stuart_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2322" data-file-height="3004" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Jay_(Gilbert_Stuart_portrait).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>&#160;&#160;&#160;<a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a><br />Negotiator of the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a></center></div></div></div>\n<p>In April 1792, the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a> broke out between Great Britain and France, and Washington declared America\xe2\x80\x99s neutrality with the approval of his cabinet. The revolutionary government of France sent diplomat <a href="/wiki/Edmond-Charles_Gen%C3%AAt" title="Edmond-Charles Gen\xc3\xaat">"Citizen Gen\xc3\xaat"</a> to America. He was welcomed with great enthusiasm and began promoting the case for France, using a network of new <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Societies" title="Democratic-Republican Societies">Democratic-Republican Societies</a> in major cities. He even issued French <a href="/wiki/Letter_of_marque" title="Letter of marque">letters of marque and reprisal</a> to French ships manned by American sailors so that they could capture British merchant ships. Washington denounced the societies and demanded that the French recall Gen\xc3\xaat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995335\xe2\x80\x9354_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995335\xe2\x80\x9354-319">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hamilton formulated the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> to normalize trade relations with Great Britain, remove them from western forts, and resolve financial debts remaining from the Revolution;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995ch._9_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995ch._9-320">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a> negotiated and signed the treaty on November 19, 1794. Jeffersonians, on the other hand, supported France and strongly attacked the treaty. Washington listened to both sides, then announced his support for the treaty, mostly because it avoided war with Britain;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010730_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010730-321">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> yet he was deeply disappointed in it and claimed that its provisions were overwhelmingly on the side of Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009340_314-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009340-314">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> He mobilized public opinion and secured ratification in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEstes2000393\xe2\x80\x93422Estes2001127\xe2\x80\x9358_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEstes2000393\xe2\x80\x93422Estes2001127\xe2\x80\x9358-322">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Washington was not immune to Republican criticism after he signed the Jay Treaty and was subjected to the worst and most frequent criticism that he had ever experienced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009344_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009344-323">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British agreed to depart from their forts around the Great Lakes, and the United States-Canada boundary had to be modified. Numerous pre-Revolutionary debts were liquidated, and the British opened their <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">West Indies colonies</a> to American trade. Most importantly, the treaty secured peace with Britain and brought a decade of prosperous trade. Jefferson claimed that it angered France and "invited rather than avoided" war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009343_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009343-324">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> Relations with France deteriorated after the Jay Treaty was signed, leaving President John Adams with the prospect of war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2005263Lengel2005357_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2005263Lengel2005357-325">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> Two days before Washington\'s term ended, the French withdrew from their embassy in America.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_term">Second term</span></h3>\n<p>Washington remained popular approaching the Election of 1792, and Hamilton urged him to run for a second term. He said nothing about this upon his return to Mount Vernon in October 1792, and many took his silence as consent and viewed him as the only viable candidate during that unstable period. The Electoral College unanimously elected him President on February 13, 1793 for a second term. John Adams was re-elected Vice President by a vote of 77 to 50.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke200210_318-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210-318">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington was criticized by the <a href="/wiki/National_Gazette" title="National Gazette">National Gazette</a> and political adversaries over his birthday celebration and also for appearing as a "monarchist", so he kept a low profile and arrived alone at his <a href="/wiki/Second_inauguration_of_George_Washington" title="Second inauguration of George Washington">inauguration</a> in a simple carriage. It was held in the Senate Chamber of <a href="/wiki/Congress_Hall" title="Congress Hall">Congress Hall</a> in Philadelphia on Monday, March 4, 1793, and the presidential oath of office was administered by Associate Justice <a href="/wiki/William_Cushing" title="William Cushing">William Cushing</a>. This was the first inauguration to take place in Philadelphia, the nation\'s capital at the time. Washington maintained his low profile after the ceremonies and delivered the shortest inaugural address on record.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010687_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010687-326">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1793, Washington signed the <a href="/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Fugitive Slave Act">Fugitive Slave Act</a> which allowed slave owners to cross state lines and retrieve runaway slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010758_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010758-327">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> He also signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_of_1794" title="Slave Trade Act of 1794">Slave Trade Act of 1794</a> which limited American involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBassett1906187\xe2\x80\x93189_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBassett1906187\xe2\x80\x93189-328">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> He appointed <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Wolcott,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Oliver Wolcott, Jr.">Oliver Wolcott, Jr.</a> as Secretary of the Treasury in 1795, replacing Alexander Hamilton who resigned in the aftermath of the whiskey rebellion for which he was criticized; he also wished to return home to his wife who suffered a miscarriage in his absence. The affair strengthened Washington\'s bond with Hamilton, while it distanced him from Knox.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010726\xe2\x80\x93727_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010726\xe2\x80\x93727-329">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington was relentlessly assailed by his political foes and a largely partisan press in the final months of his presidency, and they accused him of being ambitious and greedy. He pointed out that he had taken no salary during the entire war and risked his life in numerous battles, and he regarded the press as an erosive and disuniting force which spread numerous falsehoods, referring to them as "diabolical". This also had a great influence in his <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address" title="George Washington&#39;s Farewell Address">Farewell Address</a>, which expressed how troubled he was by the years of in-fighting and character assassination by much of the press.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997491\xe2\x80\x93492Chernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93754_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997491\xe2\x80\x93492Chernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93754-330">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington wanted to retire when his first term ended but all his advisors begged him to stand for reelection.  By 1796 he insisted on retiring, for both personal and political reasons. He felt worn out, and was disgusted with the virulent personal attacks on his integrity.  Politically he felt America needed a contested presidential election with a choice of candidates, which would not happen if he ran again. If he won and then died a vice president would take over who was not elected and his goal would fail.  (He in fact did die in 1799 two years into the new term). The election went forward and he was absolutely neutral between Adams and Jefferson. He personally did not feel bound by a two-term limit, but he relinquished power, once again giving impetus to this idea. However, Washington has been misinterpreted as the <i>founder</i> of a two-term presidential limit, but proper attribution for this custom belonged to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, who refused to run for a third term on political foundation. In the 1780s, about half the states provided term limits for governors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorzi201143_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorzi201143-331">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeabody,_2001,_pp.439-453_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeabody,_2001,_pp.439-453-332">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farewell_Address">Farewell Address</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address" title="George Washington&#39;s Farewell Address">George Washington\'s Farewell Address</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg/240px-Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg" width="240" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg/360px-Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg/480px-Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg 2x" data-file-width="931" data-file-height="557" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington%27s_Farewell_Address.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address" title="George Washington&#39;s Farewell Address">Washington\'s Farewell Address</a> (September 19, 1796)</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington had planned to retire after his first term as President, and he had <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> draft a farewell message in 1792, the sentiment and theme being Washington\'s; he then put it in a drawer of his presidential desk when he was re-elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlexner1972292Chernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93753_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1972292Chernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93753-333">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> He gave the draft to Hamilton to complete on May 15, 1796, with additional text that he had written himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93753_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93753-334">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> The final version of the <i>Farewell Address</i> was published on September 19, 1796 by David Claypool\'s <i>American Daily Advertiser</i>, and then published by three other Philadelphia newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010754_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010754-335">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> It warned against foreign alliances and their influence in domestic affairs, and against bitter partisanship in domestic politics. It also called for men to move beyond partisanship and serve the common good, <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997492_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997492-336">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> stressing that the United States must concentrate on its own interests. He counseled friendship and commerce with all nations, but advised against involvement in European wars.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFishmanPedersonRozell2001119\xe2\x80\x93120GreggSpalding1999199\xe2\x80\x93216_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFishmanPedersonRozell2001119\xe2\x80\x93120GreggSpalding1999199\xe2\x80\x93216-337">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> He stressed the importance of religion, claiming that "religion and morality are indispensable supports" in a republic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010-338">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> The address, however, did not quell bipartisan politics but served to aggravate them, setting the tone for the coming 1796 election when Washington supported John Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997492_336-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997492-336">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 7, 1796 Washington read his Eighth Annual Address to Congress. He spoke before the House, wore a black velvet suit, and donned his sword, while he was well received by "the largest assemblage of citizens" in the crowded gallery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010764_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010764-339">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> He advocated for the creation of a military academy, and expounded on the fact that the British had vacated the Northwest Forts and Algiers had released American prisoners. The speech was well received. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010764\xe2\x80\x93765_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010764\xe2\x80\x93765-340">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s Farewell Address proved to be one of the most influential statements on republicanism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93754_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010752\xe2\x80\x93754-341">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> It stressed the necessity and importance of national union, the value of the Constitution, the rule of law, the evils of political parties, and the proper virtues of a republican people. He refers to morality as "a necessary spring of popular government". "Whatever may be conceded to the influence of refined education on minds of peculiar structure, reason, and experience, both forbid us to expect that national morality can prevail in exclusion of religious principle."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoller196347_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoller196347-342">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_and_slavery" title="George Washington and slavery">George Washington and slavery</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg/280px-Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg" width="280" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg/420px-Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg/560px-Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1045" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Junius_Brutus_Stearns_-_George_Washington_as_Farmer_at_Mount_Vernon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Washington as Farmer at Mount Vernon</i><br /><a href="/wiki/Junius_Brutus_Stearns" title="Junius Brutus Stearns">Junius Brutus Stearns</a>, 1851</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington was born into a world accustomed to slavery; he had no qualms about it prior to 1775 and held traditional views that blacks were an inferior race. During the war, however, his views moderated under the influence of anti-slavery aides and officers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002163\xe2\x80\x93164Hirschfeld19972Flexner1974386_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002163\xe2\x80\x93164Hirschfeld19972Flexner1974386-343">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> He spoke often of ending slavery following the war, but he never voiced those views publicly, fearing that the issue would divide the new nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart2007257Striner200615Ferling2002275-276_344-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart2007257Striner200615Ferling2002275-276-344">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are conflicting reports of slave treatment at Mount Vernon. Washington discouraged cruelty, yet there are records of harsh punishments which included whippings being inflicted on male and female slaves by their overseers\xe2\x80\x93some of whom were also slaves. In some cases, runaway slaves were put in shackles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling200246Chernow2010Slave_Control_345-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200246Chernow2010Slave_Control-345">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> When he was President, Washington maintained distant supervision of Mount Vernon through letters to his overseers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010640Slave_Control_346-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010640Slave_Control-346">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> though there is only one account from him authorizing a whipping that was given to a slave for badly beating his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchenawolf2015_347-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchenawolf2015-347">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup> He directed that a warning be given to first offenders before resorting to whipping, which was then subject to his prior approval; this wasn\'t always enforced due to his prolonged absences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003Ferling200246Chernow2010113\xe2\x80\x93114,_117_348-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003Ferling200246Chernow2010113\xe2\x80\x93114,_117-348">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> In severe circumstances, he shipped unruly slaves to the West Indies. He also used non-violent forms of discipline, including cash payments, material incentives, and what he referred to as "admonition and advise".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlave_Control_349-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlave_Control-349">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington sometimes personally cared for ill or injured slaves, and he provided physicians and mid-wives. By the Revolutionary War, he had all his slaves inoculated for smallpox. He ordered them to work from dawn to dusk,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010111Ferling200246_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010111Ferling200246-350">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> and they were often poorly clothed and housed\xe2\x80\x94yet they were well fed and received two hours off for meals during the workday. They worked shorter days in the winter months and were given time off on Sundays, Christmas, Easter, and Pentecost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwarz200127,_83_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwarz200127,_83-351">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlave_Labor_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlave_Labor-352">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the war, Washington initially forbade blacks from becoming soldiers, but he allowed them to serve in the Continental Army beginning in January, 1776 in order to counter Loyalist <a href="/wiki/John_Murray,_4th_Earl_of_Dunmore" title="John Murray, 4th Earl of Dunmore">Governor Dunmore\'s</a> offer to free slaves if they fought for the British. After the war, Washington ceased his slave-trading and supported many slaves who were too young or too old to work, greatly increasing Mount Vernon\'s slave population and causing the plantation to operate at a loss.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld19972Ellis2004167_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld19972Ellis2004167-353">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> His manager noticed that there were more than 300 slaves at Mount Vernon, but only 100 were needed for profitable operation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003319,_348\xe2\x80\x93349Flexner1974386_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003319,_348\xe2\x80\x93349Flexner1974386-354">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup> By 1799, there were 317 slaves living at Mount Vernon; he owned 124 outright and held 153 for his wife\xe2\x80\x99s dower interest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_Washington_&amp;_Slavery&quot;_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_Washington_&amp;_Slavery&quot;-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>  At times, Mount Vernon slaves ran away to find freedom, encouraged by Quaker abolitionists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_Washington_&amp;_Slavery&quot;_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;Ten_Facts_About_Washington_&amp;_Slavery&quot;-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010637,_759\xe2\x80\x93762_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010637,_759\xe2\x80\x93762-355">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> Washington used secretive methods to return them rather than post public advertisements in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld19975,6_356-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld19975,6-356">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> For example, he privately ordered the capture of his wife\'s fugitive slave <a href="/wiki/Oney_Judge" title="Oney Judge">Oney Judge</a> in 1796.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010637,_759\xe2\x80\x93762_355-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010637,_759\xe2\x80\x93762-355">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the summer of 1799, he resolved his personal struggle with slave-holding by making a new will which directed that his 124 slaves be freed upon the death of Martha;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002277_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002277-357">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> he was among the few slave-holding Founding Fathers to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003352\xe2\x80\x93354_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek2003352\xe2\x80\x93354-358">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> He provided that old and young freed people be taken care of indefinitely; younger ones were to be taught to read and write and placed in suitable occupations. Martha freed his slaves on January 1, 1801. Historian John Ferling posits that Washington\'s will and the freeing of his slaves were "an act of atonement for a lifetime of concurrence in human exploitation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2002277_357-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002277-357">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Retirement_.281797.E2.80.931799.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement_(1797\xe2\x80\x931799)">Retirement (1797\xe2\x80\x931799)</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Post-presidency_of_George_Washington" title="Post-presidency of George Washington">Post-presidency of George Washington</a></div>\n<p>Washington retired from the presidency in March 1797 and returned to Mount Vernon with a profound sense of relief. He devoted much time to his plantations and other business interests, including his distillery which produced its first batch of spirits in February 1797.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenWhite2006209\xe2\x80\x9320_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenWhite2006209\xe2\x80\x9320-359">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> His plantation operations were only minimally profitable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow201053_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201053-360">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> His lands in the west (Piedmont) yielded little income because they were under attack by Indians, and the squatters living there refused to pay him rent. He attempted to sell off these holdings but failed to obtain the price that he desired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2004255\xe2\x80\x9361_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004255\xe2\x80\x9361-361">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> Once in retirement, he became an even more committed Federalist. He was vocal in his support of the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a> and convinced Federalist <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a> to run for Congress to weaken the Jeffersonian hold on Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974386_362-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974386-362">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington grew restless of retirement, prompted by tensions with France, and he wrote to Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/James_McHenry" title="James McHenry">James McHenry</a> offering to organize President Adams\' army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall1997497_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997497-363">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>  French privateers began seizing American ships in 1798, and relations with France deteriorated and led to the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a>. Adams offered Washington a commission as lieutenant general on July 4, 1798, and as Commander-in-chief of the armies raised for service in that conflict. He accepted and served as the Commanding General from July 13, 1798 until his death 17 months later. He participated in planning for a provisional army to meet any emergency, but he avoided involvement in details. He delegated most of the work to Hamilton, including active leadership of the army as a major general. No army invaded the United States during this period, and Washington did not assume a field command.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn1975225\xe2\x80\x9342Grizzard2005264_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1975225\xe2\x80\x9342Grizzard2005264-364">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;n&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is popularly assumed that Washington was rich because of the well-known "glorified fa\xc3\xa7ade of wealth and grandeur" at Mount Vernon,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010708_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010708-366">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> but nearly all of his wealth was tied up in land and slaves. Historians estimate that this estate was worth about $1 million in 1799 dollars, equivalent to about $19.9 million in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalzellDalzell1998219_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalzellDalzell1998219-367">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_days">Final days</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Life_of_George_Washington,_Deathbed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg/220px-Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg/330px-Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg/440px-Life_of_George_Washington%2C_Deathbed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2845" data-file-height="1970" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Life_of_George_Washington,_Deathbed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Washington on his Deathbed</i><br /><a href="/wiki/Junius_Brutus_Stearns" title="Junius Brutus Stearns">Junius Brutus Stearns</a> 1799</div></div></div>\n<p>On Thursday, December 12, 1799, Washington inspected his farms by horse while snow and sleet were falling. He returned late for dinner, his neck was wet, and snow matted his hair. He refused to change his wet clothes, not wanting to keep his guests waiting. He had a sore throat the following day but again went out in freezing, snowy weather to mark trees for cutting. That evening, he complained of a sore throat and chest congestion, but he remained cheerful. On Saturday morning, he awoke to an inflamed throat and difficulty breathing. He ordered estate overseer George Rawlins to remove nearly a pint of his blood, a common practice of the time, and several physicians<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;o&#93;</a></sup> were summoned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x9310_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x9310-370">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorens19991845-1849_371-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorens19991845-1849-371">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dr. Brown thought that Washington had "<a href="/wiki/Peritonsillar_abscess" title="Peritonsillar abscess">quinsey</a>" or "quincy", while Dick thought that the condition was a more serious "violent inflammation of the throat".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x93807Lear1799257_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x93807Lear1799257-372">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> Continued blood-letting (approximately five pints) proved unsuccessful. Dick proposed an emergency <a href="/wiki/Tracheotomy" title="Tracheotomy">tracheotomy</a>, but the other two doctors were unfamiliar with the new procedure and disapproved, so it was not used.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x9310FelisatiSperati200555\xe2\x80\x9358_373-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x9310FelisatiSperati200555\xe2\x80\x9358-373">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> Washington instructed Brown and Dick to stop their attempts to save his life and leave the room, while he assured Craik, "Doctor, I die hard, but I am not afraid to go".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2004269_374-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004269-374">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s illness and death came swiftly and unexpectedly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling2009365_375-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009365-375">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> He died peacefully with Martha calmly composed at the foot of his bed around 10 p.m. on Saturday, December 14, 1799 at age 67. His personal secretary Tobias Lear recorded his last words as "\'Tis well." Funeral arrangements were made by Washington\'s Masonic lodge of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria, Virginia</a>. When news of his death reached Congress, they immediately adjourned for the day and the Speaker\'s chair was shrouded in black the next morning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrving1869359_376-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrving1869359-376">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The funeral was held on December 18, 1799 at Mount Vernon, where his body was interred. Cavalry and foot soldiers led the procession, while six colonels served as the pallbearers. The Mount Vernon funeral service was restricted mostly to family and friends. Congress chose <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lee_III" title="Henry Lee III">Henry Lee III</a> to deliver the eulogy, a Continental Army officer and loved by Washington. Word of his death traveled slowly; church bells rang in the cities, and many places of business closed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810\xe2\x80\x93811_377-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810\xe2\x80\x93811-377">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> People worldwide admired Washington and were saddened by his death, and memorial processions were held in major cities of the United States. Martha wore a black mourning cape for one year, and she burned their correspondence to protect their privacy. Only five letters between the couple are known to have survived, two letters from Martha to George and three from him to her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010814_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010814-378">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_diagnoses">Subsequent diagnoses</span></h3>\n<p>The diagnosis of Washington\'s illness and the immediate cause of his death have been subjects of debate since the day that he died. In the days immediately following his death, Craik and Brown\'s published account<sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">&#91;p&#93;</a></sup> stated that his symptoms had been consistent with <i>cynanche trachealis</i>, a term of that period used to describe severe inflammation of the structures of the upper airway. Accusations have persisted since Washington\'s death concerning medical malpractice, with some believing that he had been bled to death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x9310FelisatiSperati200555\xe2\x80\x9358_373-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806\xe2\x80\x9310FelisatiSperati200555\xe2\x80\x9358-373">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> Various modern medical authors have speculated that he died from a severe case of <a href="/wiki/Epiglottitis" title="Epiglottitis">epiglottitis</a> complicated by the given treatments (which were all accepted medical practice in that day), most notably the massive deliberate blood loss which almost certainly caused <a href="/wiki/Hypovolemia" title="Hypovolemia">hypovolemic shock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">&#91;q&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burial">Burial</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg/220px-Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg/330px-Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg/440px-Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="1706" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_George_Washington_-_wide_-_Mount_Vernon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Washington family tomb at Mount Vernon</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Tomb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Washington_Tomb.jpg/220px-Washington_Tomb.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Washington_Tomb.jpg/330px-Washington_Tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Washington_Tomb.jpg/440px-Washington_Tomb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Tomb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The sarcophagi of George (right) and Martha (left) Washington at the present tomb\'s entrance</div></div></div>\n<p>Washington was buried in the old family vault at Mount Vernon, situated on a grassy slope covered with juniper and cypress trees. It contained the remains of his brother Lawrence and other family members, but the decrepit vault was in need of repair, prompting Washington to leave instructions in his will for the construction of a new vault.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810\xe2\x80\x93811_377-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810\xe2\x80\x93811-377">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1830, a disgruntled ex-employee of the estate <a href="/wiki/Attempted_theft_of_George_Washington%27s_head" title="Attempted theft of George Washington&#39;s head">attempted to steal</a> Washington\'s skull.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECraughwell200977\xe2\x80\x9379_385-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECraughwell200977\xe2\x80\x9379-385">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> The next year, the new vault was constructed at Mount Vernon to receive the remains of George and Martha and other relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-NewTomb_386-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewTomb-386">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup> In 1832, a joint Congressional committee debated moving his body from Mount Vernon to a crypt in the Capitol. The crypt had been built by architect <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bulfinch" title="Charles Bulfinch">Charles Bulfinch</a> in the 1820s during the reconstruction of the burned-out capital, after the <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_Washington" title="Burning of Washington">Burning of Washington</a> by the British during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>. Southern opposition was intense, antagonized by an ever-growing rift between North and South; many were concerned that Washington\'s remains could end up on "a shore foreign to his native soil" should the country become divided.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoorstin1965349\xe2\x80\x9350_387-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoorstin1965349\xe2\x80\x9350-387">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On October 7, 1837, Washington\'s remains were placed, still in the original lead coffin, within a marble sarcophagus designed by William Strickland and constructed by John Struthers earlier that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrickland184011\xe2\x80\x9314_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrickland184011\xe2\x80\x9314-388">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlson2016chapter_one_389-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlson2016chapter_one-389">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup> The sarcophagus was sealed and encased with planks, while an outer vault was constructed around it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrickland184011\xe2\x80\x9314_388-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrickland184011\xe2\x80\x9314-388">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> The outer vault has the sarcophagi of George and Martha Washington; the inner vault has the remains of other Washington family members and relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-NewTomb_386-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewTomb-386">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_reputation_and_legacy">Historical reputation and legacy</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Legacy_of_George_Washington" title="Legacy of George Washington">Legacy of George Washington</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_George_Washington" title="Cultural depictions of George Washington">Cultural depictions of George Washington</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg/170px-Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg/255px-Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg/340px-Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg 2x" data-file-width="845" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stuart-george-washington-constable-1797.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Constable-Hamilton Portrait</i> by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Washington\'s legacy endures as one of the most influential in American history, since he served as Commander-in-Chief of the Continental Army, a hero of the Revolution, and the first President of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">&#91;r&#93;</a></sup> Congressman <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lee_III" title="Henry Lee III">Henry "Light-Horse Harry" Lee</a> was a Revolutionary War comrade, and he <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Father_of_His_Country" class="extiw" title="s:The Father of His Country">famously eulogized Washington</a>: "First in war\xe2\x80\x94first in peace\xe2\x80\x94and first in the hearts of his countrymen".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling20093\xe2\x80\x934_391-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20093\xe2\x80\x934-391">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup> Lee\'s words became the hallmark by which Washington\'s overwhelming reputation was impressed upon the American memory, with biographers hailing him as the great exemplar of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>. He set many precedents for the national government and the presidency in particular, and he was called the "Father of His Country" as early as 1778.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337ParryAllison1991xiHindle201792_392-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger2013236\xe2\x80\x9337ParryAllison1991xiHindle201792-392">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">&#91;s&#93;</a></sup> Congress proclaimed Washington\'s birthday to be a federal holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling20094_395-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20094-395">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup> Biographer <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Southall_Freeman" title="Douglas Southall Freeman">Douglas Southall Freeman</a> concludes, "The great big thing stamped across that man is character." By character, says <a href="/wiki/David_Hackett_Fischer" title="David Hackett Fischer">David Hackett Fischer</a>, "Freeman meant integrity, self-discipline, courage, absolute honesty, resolve, and decision, but also forbearance, decency, and respect for others."\n<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004446_396-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004446-396">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington became an international icon for liberation and nationalism, as the leader of the first successful revolution against a colonial empire. The <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalists</a> made him the symbol of their party, but the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Jeffersonians</a> continued to distrust his influence for many years and delayed building the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe195824\xe2\x80\x9326_397-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe195824\xe2\x80\x9326-397">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> On January 31, 1781, he was elected a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillard2017_398-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillard2017-398">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial" title="United States Bicentennial">United States Bicentennial</a>, Washington was posthumously appointed to the grade of General of the Armies of the United States by the congressional joint resolution <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Public_Law_94-479" class="extiw" title="s:Public Law 94-479">Public Law 94-479</a> passed on January 19, 1976, with an effective appointment date of July 4, 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell200552,_66_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell200552,_66-252">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> This restored his place as the highest-ranking military officer in U.S. history.<sup id="cite_ref-400" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-400">&#91;t&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most popular childhood story about Washington is that he chopped down his father\'s favorite cherry tree and admitted the deed when questioned: "I cannot tell a lie".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENovak20078_401-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENovak20078-401">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup> The anecdote was first reported by biographer <a href="/wiki/Parson_Weems" class="mw-redirect" title="Parson Weems">Parson Weems</a>, based on the memories of people who knew him as a child.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHughes19261:24,_501Grizzard200245\xe2\x80\x9347_402-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHughes19261:24,_501Grizzard200245\xe2\x80\x9347-402">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup> However, Weems never asserted that the tree was actually chopped down, but that the young Washington instead had merely removed some of the tree bark with his hatchet.<sup id="cite_ref-404" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-404">&#91;u&#93;</a></sup> \nWeems\' account of the incident has never been proven or disproven.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevy20136,_217Weems191822_405-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy20136,_217Weems191822-405">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monuments_and_memorials">Monuments and memorials</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg/170px-Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg/255px-Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg/340px-Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1984" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Monument_Dusk_Jan_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a>, Washington, DC</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_memorials_to_George_Washington" title="List of memorials to George Washington">List of memorials to George Washington</a></div>\n<p>Many places and monuments have been named in honor of Washington, most notably the nation\'s capital. The state of Washington is the only state to be named after a president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;Washington&quot;._Worldatlas_406-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;Washington&quot;._Worldatlas-406">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Bridge" title="George Washington Bridge">George Washington Bridge</a> is named in his honor, as are several naval vessels, including the nuclear-powered aircraft carrier <a href="/wiki/USS_George_Washington_(CVN-73)" title="USS George Washington (CVN-73)">USS George Washington (CVN-73)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;USS_George_Washington&quot;_407-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;USS_George_Washington&quot;-407">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, Thomas Jefferson, and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> are depicted in stone at the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rushmore" title="Mount Rushmore">Mount Rushmore</a> Memorial. The <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a> was built in his honor and the <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Masonic_National_Memorial" title="George Washington Masonic National Memorial">George Washington Masonic National Memorial</a> in Alexandria, Virginia was constructed between 1922 and 1932 with contributions from the <a href="/wiki/Freemasons" class="mw-redirect" title="Freemasons">Freemasons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECallahan1998329\xe2\x80\x93342Weber2009137_408-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECallahan1998329\xe2\x80\x93342Weber2009137-408">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Washington\'s death, Congress authorized a memorial in the national capital, but the decision was reversed when the Democratic-Republicans took control of Congress in 1801.
