The Democratic-Republicans were dismayed that Washington had become the symbol of the Federalist Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1991187\xe2\x80\x93203_409-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1991187\xe2\x80\x93203-409">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> Construction of the 555-foot (169 m) memorial was further delayed by politics and funding problems; it did not begin until 1848 and was not completed until 1885\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<center><b>Statues and sculptures</b></center>\n<center><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Indy_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Washington_Indy_Hall.jpg/90px-Washington_Indy_Hall.jpg" width="90" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Washington_Indy_Hall.jpg/135px-Washington_Indy_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Washington_Indy_Hall.jpg/180px-Washington_Indy_Hall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1846" data-file-height="2462" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_A._Bailly" title="Joseph A. Bailly">Bailly\'s</a> George Washington, <a href="/wiki/Independence_Hall" title="Independence Hall">Independence Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument_(Baltimore)" title="Washington Monument (Baltimore)">Washington Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Virginia_State_Capitol_complex_-_Houdon%27s_Washington,_seen_from_the_front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Antoine_Houdon" title="Jean-Antoine Houdon">Jean-Antoine Houdon</a>\'s statue, <a href="/wiki/Virginia_State_Capitol" title="Virginia State Capitol">State Capitol</a> in Virginia\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Mt. Rushmore, George Washington closeup\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul></center>\n<center><b>Postage and Currency</b></center><p><br /></p><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps#George_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. presidents on U.S. postage stamps">U.S. presidents on U.S. postage stamps \xc2\xa7&#160;George Washington</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Virginia_on_stamps" title="History of Virginia on stamps">History of Virginia on stamps</a></div>\n<p>George Washington appears on contemporary U.S. currency, including the <a href="/wiki/United_States_one-dollar_bill" title="United States one-dollar bill">one-dollar bill</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin)" title="Quarter (United States coin)">quarter-dollar coin</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Washington_quarter" title="Washington quarter">Washington quarter</a>).\nWashington and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> appeared on the <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamps_and_postal_history_of_the_United_States#First_national_postage_stamps" title="Postage stamps and postal history of the United States">nation\'s first postage stamps</a> in 1847. Since that time, Washington has appeared on many postage issues, more than all other presidents combined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKloetzel2009_410-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKloetzel2009-410">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<center><b>Selected issues</b></center>\n<table>\n\n<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">\n<td><div class="thumb id="Papers">Papers</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Papers_of_George_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="The Papers of George Washington">The Papers of George Washington</a></div>\n<p>The serious collection and publication of Washington\'s documentary record began with the pioneer work of <a href="/wiki/Jared_Sparks" title="Jared Sparks">Jared Sparks</a> in the 1830s in <i>Life and Writings of George Washington</i> (12 vols., 1834\xe2\x80\x931837).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESparks1839Title_page_411-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESparks1839Title_page-411">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Writings of George Washington from the Original Manuscript Sources, 1745\xe2\x80\x931799</i> (1931\xe2\x80\x9344) is a 39-volume set edited by <a href="/wiki/John_Clement_Fitzpatrick" title="John Clement Fitzpatrick">John Clement Fitzpatrick</a> who was commissioned by the <i>George Washington Bicentennial Commission</i>. It contains over 17,000 letters and documents and is available online from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick2016_412-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick2016-412">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup> The definitive letterpress edition of his writings was begun by the University of Virginia in 1968, and today comprises 52 published volumes with more to come. It contains everything written by Washington or signed by him, together with most of his incoming letters. Part of the collection is available online from the University of Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELengel2011_413-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2011-413">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Washington\'s personal annotated copy of the "Acts Passed at a Congress of the United States of America" from 1789 includes the Constitution of the United States and a draft of the Bill of Rights. It was sold at <a href="/wiki/Christie%27s" title="Christie&#39;s">Christie\'s</a> to <a href="/wiki/The_Mount_Vernon_Ladies%27_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="The Mount Vernon Ladies&#39; Association">The Mount Vernon Ladies\' Association</a> on June 22, 2012 for $9,826,500 (plus fees). This was the record for a document sold at auction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;NYC_Auction_Of_George_Washington_Document_Sets_Record&quot;_414-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;NYC_Auction_Of_George_Washington_Document_Sets_Record&quot;-414">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portraits_gallery">Portraits alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/P_vip.svg/28px-P_vip.svg.png" width="28" height="28" class="noviewer" portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Electoral_history_of_George_Washington" title="Electoral history of George Washington">Electoral history of George Washington</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Culper_Ring" title="Culper Ring">Culper Ring</a>, the spy ring organized by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Tallmadge" title="Benjamin Tallmadge">Benjamin Tallmadge</a> and supervised by Washington</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/British_Army_during_the_American_War_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="British Army during the American War of Independence">British Army during the American War of Independence</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Town_Destroyer" title="Town Destroyer">Conotocaurious (Town Destroyer)</a>, a nickname given to Washington by Iroquois Native Americans</li></ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">March 4 is the official start of the first presidential term. April 6 is when Congress counted the votes of the Electoral College and certified a president. April 30 is when Washington was sworn in.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Old style: February 11, 1731</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-calendar-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-calendar_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Contemporaneous records used the Julian calendar and the <a href="/wiki/New_Year#Historical_Christian_new_year_dates" title="New Year">Annunciation Style</a> of enumerating years, recording his birth as February 11, 1731. The provisions of the British <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">Calendar (New Style) Act 1750</a>, implemented in 1752, altered the official British dating method to the Gregorian calendar with the start of the year on January 1 (it had been March 25). These changes resulted in dates being moved forward 11 days, and an advance of one year for those between January 1 and March 25. For a further explanation, see <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">Old Style and New Style dates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngber2006_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngber2006-4">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Three siblings died before adulthood: sister Mildred at age one, half-brother Butler in infancy, and half-sister Jane at age 12.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_509\xe2\x80\x93510Chernow20106\xe2\x80\x938_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936V19:_509\xe2\x80\x93510Chernow20106\xe2\x80\x938-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ellis and Ferling, for example, point to his negative experiences dealing with the Continental Congress during the Revolution, and Don Higginbotham places Washington\'s first formal advocacy of a strong central government in 1783.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHigginbotham200237_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHigginbotham200237-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Washington secretly instructed Captain William Crawford of the Ohio Country to scout out forbidden lands in the 1760s, beyond the Kings\' Royal Proclamation Line.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010148_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010148-126">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a letter of September 20, 1765, Washington protested to <i>Robert Cary &amp; Co.</i> of the low prices that he received for his tobacco, and for the inflated prices that he was forced to pay on second-rate goods from London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2010138_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010138-129">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Congress initially attempted to direct the war effort in June, 1776 with the committee known as "Board of War and Ordnance"; this was succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_War" title="Board of War">Board of War</a> in July 1777, which eventually included members of the military.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedman200842_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman200842-154">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-170">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This painting has received both acclaim and criticism;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowat1968290,_293,_297Nowlan201266_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowat1968290,_293,_297Nowlan201266-169">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> see <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Leutze" title="Emanuel Leutze">Emanuel Leutze</a> article for details.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-180"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-180">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Durham Boats</i> were built for the Durham Iron Works to carry iron ore, lumber, or grain on the upper Delaware.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer2004216_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004216-179">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-263"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-263">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Starting in 1774, 14 men served as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Continental_Congress" title="President of the Continental Congress">President of the Continental Congress</a> but bore no relationship to the presidency established under Article II of the Constitution. Under the Articles of Confederation, Congress called its presiding officer "President of the United States in Congress Assembled", but this position had no national executive powers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJensen1948178\xe2\x80\x93179Unger201361,_146_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJensen1948178\xe2\x80\x93179Unger201361,_146-261">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJillsonWilson199477_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJillsonWilson199477-262">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-271"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-271">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There has been debate and controversy over whether Washington added "so help me God" to the end of the oath.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrving1869475Alden1993236_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrving1869475Alden1993236-270">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-277"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-277">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Washington wrote to James Madison: "As the first of everything in our situation will serve to establish a precedent, it is devoutly wished on my part that these precedents be fixed on true principles." Washington to James Madison, May 5, 1789, cited by Unger, 2013, p. 76.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-365"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-365">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He was the only former President who served as a presidential appointee until 1921, when President <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> appointed former President <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> as Chief Justice of the United States.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-369"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-369">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Doctors <a href="/wiki/James_Craik" title="James Craik">James Craik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustavus_Richard_Brown" title="Gustavus Richard Brown">Gustavus Richard Brown</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elisha_C._Dick" title="Elisha C. Dick">Elisha C. Dick</a>. (<a href="/wiki/Dr._William_Thornton" class="mw-redirect" title="Dr. William Thornton">Dr. William Thornton</a> arrived some hours after Washington died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&quot;Death_Defied&quot;_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&quot;Death_Defied&quot;-368">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup>)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-380"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-380">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first account of Washington\'s death was written by Doctors Craik and Brown, published in <i>The Times</i> of Alexandria five days after his death on December 19, 1799. It can be found in <i>The Eclectic Medical Journal</i> (1858)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewtonFreemanBickley1858273\xe2\x80\x93274_379-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewtonFreemanBickley1858273\xe2\x80\x93274-379">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-384"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-384">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Modern experts have concluded that Washington probably died from acute bacterial epiglottitis complicated by the administered treatments, including Morens and Wallenborn in 1999,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWallenborn1999_381-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallenborn1999-381">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorens19991845-1849_371-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorens19991845-1849-371">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Cheatham in 2008, <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheatham2008_382-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheatham2008-382">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> and Vadakan in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVadakan2005_383-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVadakan2005-383">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> These treatments included multiple doses of <a href="/wiki/Mercury(I)_chloride" title="Mercury(I) chloride">calomel</a> (a cathartic or purgative) and extensive bloodletting.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-390"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-390">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historians <a href="/wiki/Jay_A._Parry" title="Jay A. Parry">Jay A. Parry</a> and Andrew M. Allison declare that Washington "was the dominant personality in three of the most critical events in that founding: the Revolutionary War, the Constitutional Convention, and the first national administration. Had he not served as America\'s leader in those three events, all three likely would have failed. And America as we know it today would not exist." Parry, 1991, p. xi.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-394"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-394">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The earliest known image in which Washington is identified as the Father of His Country is in the frontispiece of a 1779 German-language almanac, with calculations by <a href="/wiki/David_Rittenhouse" title="David Rittenhouse">David Rittenhouse</a> and published by Francis Bailey in Lancaster County Pennsylvania. <i>Der Gantz Neue Verbesserte Nord-Americanische Calendar</i> has Fame appearing with an image of Washington holding a trumpet to her lips, from which come the words "<i>Der Landes Vater</i>" (translated as "the father of the country" or "the father of the land").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELightnerReeder1953133_393-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELightnerReeder1953133-393">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-400"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-400">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <i>Portraits &amp; Biographical Sketches of the United States Army\'s Senior Officer</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell200552_399-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell200552-399">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup> William Gardner Bell states that Washington was recalled back into military service from his retirement in 1798, and "Congress passed legislation that would have made him General of the Armies of the United States, but his services were not required in the field and the appointment was not made until the Bicentennial in 1976, when it was bestowed posthumously as a commemorative honor." <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.history.army.mil/html/faq/5star.html">How many U.S. Army five-star generals have there been and who were they?</a> states that President Ford specified that Washington would "rank first among all officers of the Army, past and present."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-404"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-404">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Weems\' story was published in 1806 and reads: "One day, in the garden, where he often amused himself hacking his mother\'s pea-sticks, he unluckily tried the edge of his hatchet on the body of a beautiful young English cherry-tree, which he barked so terribly, that I don\'t believe the tree ever got the better of it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeems191822_403-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeems191822-403">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div
